الكلام في إمامة أبي بكر : وإذا ثبت بما تقدم من وجوب الإمامة والشرائط التي من اتصف بها صلح للإمامة نقول بعد ذلك : إن أبا بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه كان بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم خليفة محقا وإمامًا حقا مفترض الطاعة واجب الاتباع فيما يورده ويصدره ويأمر به وينهى عنه. وذلك لأنه رضي الله عنه استجمع شرائط صحة الخلافة، فإنه كان قرشيًا لا ريبة لأحد فيه، وكان من وراء ذلك من العلم والديانة والصلابة ورباطة الجأش، والعلم بتدابير الحروب، والقيام بتهيئة الجيوش، وتنفيذ السرايا ومعرفة سياسة العامة، وتسوية أمور الرعية، كله كان ثابتا ولهذا اختارته الصحابة، وانقادوا لأوامره على ما نبين، ولو لم يكن من بركة أيامه ويُمن تعينه إلا الارتفاع ما وقع من الاختلاف بين الصحابة لكان ذلك دليلاً كافيًا على كونه محقا فيما تولى هاديًا مهديًا فيما يفعل ويأمر.

فإن أول خلاف وقع بين الأمة كان الخلاف في وفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فإن عمر رضي الله عنه كان يقول : إنه عليه السلام لم يمت وإن من قال إنه مات أفعل به كذا وكذا، إلى أن أتى أبو بكر الصديق وأخبر أنه مات وتلا قوله تعالى: {إِنَّكَ مَيِّتٌ وَإِنَّهُم مَّيِّتُونَ} ثم صعد المنبر وخطب وقال في خطبته: ألا من كان يعبد محمدًا فإن محمدًا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت فاجتمع الناس على موته عليه السلام.

ثم اختلفوا في موضع دفنه فأشار المهاجرون إلى أن يدفن بمكة، لأنها منشؤه وبلد آبائه، وبعضهم أشاروا إلى نقله إلى مقابر الأنبياء ببيت المقدس، والأنصار قالوا إنه يدفن بالمدينة التي هي دار هجرته ومكان ظهور ملته وانتشار دعوته إلى أن روى أبو بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه: "الأنبياء يدفنون حيث يقبضون" فقبلوا روايته وارتفع الخلاف ببركته.

ثم اختلفوا في أمر الخلافة فكان الأنصار يقولون : منا أمير ومنكم أمير، وبعضهم كانوا يميلون إلى العباس، وبعضهم يرون عليا رضي الله عنه أحق بذلك، ثم ارتفع الخلاف واتفقت الآراء واجتمعت على أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، إما لما علموا بإشارة الكتاب أو الأحاديث على ما نبين وإما استدلالاً منهم بتفويض النبي عليه السلام إقامة ما هو من أعظم أركان الدين وهو الصلاة إليه، وأمره إياه بأن يحج بالناس سنة تسع عند قعوده عليه السلام عن إقامته بنفسه لعارض شغل له، وأو بأن اللطيف الخبير جل ثناؤه وتباركت أسماؤه نظر لأمة حبيبه ومتبعي صفيه ونجيبه جمع آراءهم المختلفة وأهواءهم المشتتة على من هو أكثرهم فضلاً، وأعزهم علمًا، وأوفرهم عقلاً، وأصوبهم تدبيراً وأربطهم عند الملمات جاشا ، وأشدهم على وعد الله تعالى بإظهار الدين على الأديان اتكالاً، وأيمنهم نقيبة، وأعودهم على افتاء الخلق وطبقات الرعايا نفعًا، وأظلفهم عن الفواحش نفسا ، وأصونهم عن القبائح عرضا، وأشدهم لأمور الدين تعظيمًا، وأقدمهم إسلاما ، وأجودهم كفا، وأسمحهم، يبذل ما
The discussion on the Imamate of Abu Bakr: When it has been established by what preceded of the obligation of the Imamate and the conditions by which whoever is described with them is suitable for the Imamate, we say after that: Abu Bakr as-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him, was after the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, a rightful caliph and a true Imam, obedience to whom is obligatory, and following him is required in what he presents and issues and commands and forbids. That is because he, may Allah be pleased with him, possessed all the conditions of the validity of the caliphate, for he was a Qurayshiite about whom no one had any doubt, and besides that he had knowledge, piety, firmness, steadfastness, knowledge of military strategies, undertaking the preparation of armies, executing military expeditions, knowledge of public administration, and settling the affairs of the subjects; all of this was established, and for this the Companions chose him and submitted to his orders as we will explain. And if there were no blessing of his days and good fortune of his appointment except the removal of the disagreement that occurred among the Companions, that would be sufficient proof that he was rightful in what he undertook, a guide and rightly guided in what he does and commands. For the first disagreement that occurred among the community was the disagreement about the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him. For 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, used to say that he, peace be upon him, had not died, and that whoever said he had died he would do such and such to him, until Abu Bakr as-Siddiq came and informed them that he had died and recited the saying of Allah Almighty: {Indeed, you are to die, and indeed, they are to die} (Az-Zumar 39:30). Then he ascended the pulpit and gave a sermon, saying in his sermon: "Lo, whoever used to worship Muhammad, then Muhammad has died; and whoever used to worship the Lord of Muhammad, then the Lord of Muhammad is Alive and does not die." So the people agreed upon his death, peace be upon him. Then they disagreed about the place of his burial. The Muhajirun suggested that he be buried in Mecca because it was his birthplace and the land of his forefathers, and some of them suggested transferring him to the graves of the prophets in Jerusalem. The Ansar said that he should be buried in Medina, which was his city of emigration and the place of the emergence of his religion and the spread of his call, until Abu Bakr as-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him, narrated: "The prophets are buried where they die." So they accepted his narration, and the disagreement was removed by his blessing. Then they disagreed about the matter of the caliphate. The Ansar said: "From us an amir and from you an amir." Some of them inclined towards al-'Abbas, and some considered 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, more deserving of that. Then the disagreement was removed and the opinions agreed and united upon Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, either because they knew an indication from the Book or the hadiths as we will explain, or by their deduction from the Prophet's, peace be upon him, delegation to him of what is among the greatest pillars of the religion, namely prayer, and his order to him to lead the people in the pilgrimage in the year nine when he, peace be upon him, stayed behind from leading it himself due to an obstacle that occupied him, or because the Kind and All-Aware, Glorified is His praise and Blessed are His names, looked after the community of His beloved and the followers of His chosen intimate and united their differing opinions and scattered inclinations upon the one who was the most excellent among them, the most knowledgeable, the most complete in intellect, the most correct in administration, the firmest in heart at calamities, the most reliant on the promise of Allah Almighty to manifest the religion over all religions, the most blessed in disposition, the most beneficial in giving legal rulings to the people and the strata of subjects, the most chaste in soul from immoralities, the most protective of honor from evil deeds, the most reverent of religious matters, the earliest in Islam, the most generous in hand, the most liberal, giving what...
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the caliphate of Abu Bakr as-Siddiq. It belongs to the genre of kalam discussions on imama. The author proceeds in three steps. First, he states the claim: given that imama is obligatory and has conditions, Abu Bakr was the rightful caliph after the Prophet (saws), possessing all qualifications: Qurayshi lineage, knowledge, piety, steadfastness, military and administrative competence, etc. He specifically notes that the Companions chose him and obeyed him, and that even the removal of disagreement among them is sufficient proof of his rightness. Second, he recounts the three disputes that arose after the Prophet's death and how Abu Bakr resolved each. The first dispute was about the Prophet's death itself: 'Umar initially denied it, but Abu Bakr affirmed it by reciting the Quranic verse (39:30) and delivering a sermon that united the community. The second dispute was about the burial place: Muhajirun suggested Mecca, some suggested Jerusalem, and Ansar suggested Medina; Abu Bakr resolved it by narrating a hadith that prophets are buried where they die, and the community accepted. The third dispute was about the caliphate: Ansar proposed a shared leadership, some inclined to al-Abbas, others to Ali; but the community eventually united on Abu Bakr, either by explicit scriptural indications (to be explained later) or by deduction from the Prophet's delegation of leading prayer and pilgrimage to Abu Bakr, or by divine providence that gathered them upon the most excellent. The author then lists numerous virtues of Abu Bakr to emphasize his superiority. The passage functions as both a historical narrative and a doctrinal proof for Sunni imama.

Key Terms
وفاة
دفن
خلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Umar's denial of the Prophet's death was a sign of disbelief, but it was a moment of shock and love.
Correction: The text shows Umar was overwhelmed but later accepted Abu Bakr's evidence.

Checkpoint
How did Abu Bakr resolve the dispute about the Prophet's death?
A) He brought witnesses from the Companions.
B) He recited a Quranic verse about death and gave a sermon.
C) He appointed a committee to investigate.
D) He consulted the Ansar and Muhajirun.

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's application of Quran and hadith resolved the first two disputes, demonstrating his divinely guided leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in a disagreement within your community, do you actively seek resolution through the Quran and Sunnah, or do you cling to your own opinion? What would it take for you to put your pride aside for the sake of unity?
?
Umar's initial denial came from his deep love for the Prophet. Is there any person or thing you love so much that you struggle to accept Allah's decree regarding them? How can you train your heart to submit fully, even when it hurts?
?
Abu Bakr, through his calm knowledge, brought peace to the entire community. In your own circle—family, friends, or mosque—are you a source of healing or a source of conflict? What specific action will you take this week to be a peacemaker like Abu Bakr?

The Caliphate Dispute and the Virtues of Abu Bakr
The author narrates the resolution of the caliphate dispute and lists Abu Bakr's virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dispute over the caliphate was resolved in favor of Abu Bakr and the virtues that qualified him for leadership.

Core Idea
Through divine providence and Abu Bakr's merits, the community united upon him as caliph.

The third and most important dispute was about who should succeed the Prophet. The Ansar proposed a shared leadership, while some favored al-Abbas or Ali. Ultimately, the community agreed on Abu Bakr. The author attributes this to scriptural indications, the Prophet's delegation of prayer and pilgrimage to Abu Bakr, and divine providence guiding the people to the most excellent. This lesson also presents a list of Abu Bakr's virtues that the author began to enumerate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

The passage is incomplete; the list cuts off.

Identify the factors

The author presents these as possible reasons.
3
Examine the virtues
The author lists many virtues of Abu Bakr: most excellent, knowledgeable, intelligent, good administrator, firm, reliant on Allah, etc. Reflect on how these qualify him.
This is a typical kalam argument: the most qualified should lead.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one reason the community united on Abu Bakr?
A) He was the wealthiest among them.
B) The Prophet explicitly appointed him in a hadith.
C) The Prophet delegated leading prayer to him.
D) He was the oldest Companion.

Key Takeaway
The community's consensus on Abu Bakr arose from divine guidance and his manifest virtues, confirming his rightful caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah guided the early community to unite on Abu Bakr? If you hold this belief, does it shape how you view the history of Islam? What would it take for you to trust that even today, Allah guides the affairs of the Muslims in ways you may not understand?
?
The passage lists many virtues of Abu Bakr: knowledge, intellect, firmness, reliance on Allah, etc. Honestly, which of these virtues do you see lacking in yourself? What is the one practical step you can take this week to cultivate that virtue, knowing that it was these qualities that made him the chosen leader?
?
If divine providence resolved the caliphate dispute, what does that imply about your own disagreements with fellow Muslims? Are you willing to submit your own opinions to the possibility that Allah's plan may be different from your preferences? What would that look like in your next conflict?

Abu Bakr's Firm Execution of Prophetic Orders
Praise of Abu Bakr and historical account of his firmness in executing the Prophet's orders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Bakr's unwavering commitment to carrying out the Prophet's commands, even amid disagreement, demonstrated his leadership and earned the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr would not untie any knot the Prophet tied.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla praises Abu Bakr al-Ṣiddīq for his fearless devotion to Allah and his decisive leadership after the Prophet's death. It highlights two critical moments—the dispatch of Usama's army and the collection of zakat—where he insisted on executing the Prophet's orders without compromise. As you read, notice how his firmness led to the Companions' unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from eulogy to historical anecdote.
2
Identify the two decisions
The passage mentions two key issues after the Prophet's death: the dispatch of Usama's army and the withholding of zakat. Why were these controversial?
3
Analyze Abu Bakr's statements
Examine his two quotes: 'I will not untie a knot that the Prophet tied' and 'I would fight them for a she-goat or a rope.' What do these show about his understanding of prophetic authority?
Think about the strength of his conviction.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The passage concludes that all companions submitted to his command and assisted him. What does this imply about his leadership and the unity of the early community?
This consensus (ijma) is an important concept in Sunni theology.

He amassed wealth for the sake of God, giving generously, and he was the least concerned among them for the blame of a blamer or the disputation of an ignorant person. May God be pleased with him and grant him peace, and upon his followers and lovers, and may the curse of God, the angels, and all people be upon his haters. And similarly they differed concerning the dispatch of the army of Usama ibn Zayd and concerning the withholding of charity (zakat). And he said concerning the chapter of charity: 'By God, if they withhold from me a young she-goat or a rope that they used to pay to the Messenger of God, I would fight them for it.' Then they all submitted to his command and assisted him in his opinion.

Commentary
This passage begins with a eulogy praising someone (likely Abu Bakr al-Siddiq) for his generosity and indifference to blame in God's cause, and asks for God's pleasure upon him and his followers, and curses his haters. It then transitions to a historical account: after the Prophet's death, there was disagreement regarding two key decisions of Abu Bakr—sending out Usama ibn Zayd's army (which the Prophet had ordered before his death) and fighting those who withheld zakat. Abu Bakr insisted on executing the Prophet's orders without change, famously saying he would not untie any knot the Prophet had tied, and that he would fight even if they withheld something as small as a young she-goat or a rope. The passage concludes that all the companions ultimately complied and supported his view, indicating their consensus on his leadership.

Qur'anic Proof for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Qur'anic proof from the verse about being called to fight a people of great might.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Qur'anic verse about fighting a people of great might is used as evidence for the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Core Idea
A single verse can prove two caliphates through complementary interpretations.

This lesson explores a subtle Qur'anic argument from Surah al-Fath that early theologians used to affirm the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The verse, which speaks of being called to fight a 'people of great might,' is interpreted to identify the caller as one whose obedience is obligatory—a key attribute of the caliph. By examining two different interpretations of who those people were, the argument shows that both Abu Bakr and Umar are affirmed as caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qur'anic citation
Read the opening verse (48:16) and the author's introductory remark. Notice the conditional promise of reward and punishment.
The verse sets up the scenario.
2
Identify the logical indicator
The passage argues that the conditional reward and punishment indicate the caller is one whose obedience is obligatory (mufarrad al-ta'a). Locate this reasoning.
This is the key premise.
3
Analyze the two interpretations
The text presents two views on the identity of the 'people of great might': Banu Hanifa or the Persians. Consider the implications of each.
Both interpretations lead to the same conclusion.
4
Follow the chain of reasoning
If the people are Banu Hanifa, the caller is Abu Bakr; if Persians, the caller is Umar. Then see how Umar's caliphate implies Abu Bakr's, and vice versa.
This is the elegant argument that both caliphates are established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل من القرآن على خلافة أبي بكر] ثم الدليل من كتاب الله تعالى على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لِّلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ سَتُدْعَونَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ نُقَتِلُونَهُمْ أَوْ يُسْلِمُونَ ﴾ أمر الله تعالى نبيه عليه السلام أن يقول للذين تخلفوا عن الغزو معه أنكم ستدعون إلى قوم أولي بأس شديد، وأشار في الآية إلى أن الداعي مفترض الطاعة ينالون الثواب بطاعتهم إياه وإجابتهم إلى ما دعاهم إليه، ويستحقون التعذيب بالعذاب الأليم بعصيانهم إياه وإعراضهم عن الإجابة إلى ما دعاهم إليه، فإنه تعالى قال: ﴿ فَإِن تُطِيعُوا يُؤْتِكُمُ اللَّهُ أَجْرًا حَسَنًا وَإِن تَتَوَلَّوْا كَمَا تَوَلَّيْتُم مِّن قَبْلُ يُعَذِّبْكُمْ عَذَابًا أَلِيمًا ﴾ وهذا هو أمارة كون الداعي مفترض الطاعة، ثم سلف الأمة اختلفوا في المراد بقوله تعالى: أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ منهم من قال: بنو حنيفة ومنهم من قال: هم أهل فارس على ما قال تعالى في آية أخرى بعثنا عليْكُمْ عِبَادًا لَنَا أُولِي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ ﴾ والمراد منه أهل فارس وهم جنود بخت نصر، فإن كان المراد منه بني حنيفة فقد كان الداعي إليهم أبا بكر انه فثبتت بذلك خلافته، وإذا ثبتت خلافته ثبتت خلافة من عقد هو له الخلافة واستخلفه بعده وهو عمر الله، وإن كان المراد منه أهل فارس فالداعي إليهم كان عمر بالآية خلافته، وبثبوت خلافته ثبتت خلافة من كان هو خليفة له عقد هو له و وهو أبو بكر الله، فكان في الآية دليل خلافة الشيخين.
The proof from the Qur'an for the caliphate of Abu Bakr. Then the proof from the Book of God Almighty for that is His saying: 'Say to the bedouins who were left behind: You will be called to a people of great might; you will fight them or they will submit.' God Almighty commanded His Prophet, peace be upon him, to say to those who had lagged behind from fighting with him: 'You will be called to a people of great might.' And He indicated in the verse that the caller is one whose obedience is obligatory; they gain reward by obeying him and responding to what he calls them to, and they deserve painful punishment by disobeying him and turning away from responding to what he calls them to. For He said: 'Then if you obey, God will give you a good reward; but if you turn away as you turned away before, He will punish you with a painful punishment.' And this is an indication that the caller is one whose obedience is obligatory. Then the early generations of the community differed concerning the meaning of His saying 'of great might.' Some said: Banu Hanifa. Others said: the people of Persia, as He said in another verse: 'We sent against you servants of Ours, of great might,' and the intended meaning is the people of Persia, who were the armies of Nebuchadnezzar. If the intended meaning is Banu Hanifa, then the caller to them was Abu Bakr, and thus his caliphate is established; and if his caliphate is established, then the caliphate of the one for whom he arranged the caliphate and whom he appointed as successor after him—namely, Umar—is also established. And if the intended meaning is the people of Persia, then the caller to them was Umar, and his caliphate is established by the verse; and by the establishment of his caliphate, the caliphate of the one whom he succeeded—namely, Abu Bakr, who appointed him—is established. So in the verse is evidence for the caliphate of the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar).
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage presents a Qur'anic argument for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. It begins by citing Surah al-Fath (48:16), where God tells the Prophet to inform those who stayed behind that they will be called to fight a people of great might. The key point is that the caller is described as one whose obedience is obligatory, based on the conditional promise of reward for obedience and punishment for disobedience. The early community differed on who these 'people of great might' were: either Banu Hanifa (the tribe of Musaylimah) or the Persians (as in another verse referring to Nebuchadnezzar's armies). The argument then uses both interpretations: if Banu Hanifa, the caller must be Abu Bakr (who fought them), so his caliphate is proven; if the Persians, the caller is Umar (who led the conquest of Persia), so Umar's caliphate is proven—but since Umar was appointed by Abu Bakr, the caliphate of Abu Bakr is also necessarily established. Thus, regardless of interpretation, the verse supports the caliphate of both Abu Bakr and Umar.

Key Terms
خلافة
أبو بكر
عمر
مفترض الطاعة
أولي بأس شديد
بنو حنيفة
أهل فارس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse directly names Abu Bakr as caliph.
Correction: The verse does not name any individual; the argument infers the caller's identity through exegetical interpretation of the 'people of great might.'

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation in this passage, who are the 'people of great might'?
A) The Byzantines
B) Banu Hanifa
C) The Quraysh
D) The Jews of Khaybar

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse about being called to fight a people of great might, through its two interpretations, provides evidence for the caliphate of both Abu Bakr and Umar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, are there authorities (parents, leaders, scholars) whom you know God has commanded you to obey, but you find yourself resisting? What is the root of that resistance, and how can you align your heart with the promise of reward and fear of punishment mentioned in this verse?
?
The passage uses two different interpretations of one verse to confirm the same truth. Are you willing to accept multiple valid understandings when they all lead to a shared core belief, or do you insist on a single perspective? How might that affect your unity with fellow believers?
?
When you read verses like this that talk about obedience and punishment, do you genuinely feel a sense of accountability in your daily choices, or do you treat them as mere historical information? What would it look like for you to live as if every command from God's book carried immediate weight?

Qur'anic Evidence for Abu Bakr's Leadership from Surah Al Imran
Proof from Surah Al Imran 3:144 for Abu Bakr's leadership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about turning back on one's heels (Surah Al Imran 3:144) is used by Maturidi theologians to support Abu Bakr's caliphate and the concept of the 'thankful'.

Core Idea
The thankful are those who remained steadfast, and their leader was Abu Bakr.

This passage presents a second Qur'anic argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate, building on the first (the 'ulil amr' argument). It cites Surah Al Imran 3:144, which warns against apostasy if the Prophet dies, and interprets the 'thankful' as those who fought the apostates. The proof lies in showing that the historical events matched the verse's description, and Abu Bakr led the thankful.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its English translation. Notice the warning against turning back and the promise of reward for the thankful.
The verse is quoted in full in the passage.
2
Understand the two groups
The passage explains that the verse distinguishes between those who turn back (apostates) and those who are thankful (steadfast believers). Identify each group.
3
Consider al-Hasan's identification
Al-Hasan al-Basri is quoted as saying that Abu Bakr was the imam of the thankful. Reflect on why he made this connection.
Al-Hasan's authority is used to strengthen the argument.
4
Connect to historical context
The passage argues that the verse's mention of turning back at the Prophet's death was fulfilled in the apostasy wars during Abu Bakr's caliphate. See how this historical link is made.
No apostasy occurred before Abu Bakr's time, according to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل عليه قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَفَإِيْن مَاتَ أَوْ قُتِلَ انقَلَبْتُمْ عَلَى أَعْقَبِكُمْ وَمَن يَنقَلِبْ عَلَى عَقِبَيْهِ فَلَن يَضُرَّ اللَّهَ شَيْئًا وَسَيَجْزِي اللَّهُ الشَّاكِرِينَ ﴾ فأخبر عز وجل أن فيهم من ينقلب على عقبيه وفيهم من يشكر الله بالصبر على دينه والقيام به وقال الحسن: كان أبو بكر الله إمام الشاكرين، لأن الذين صبروا على دين الله هم الذين حاربوا المنقلبين فكان هو إمامهم، وكان ذكر الانقلاب من الله تعالى عند موت نبيه عليه السلام، فثبت أنه كان في عهد أبي بكر إذ لا ارتداد كان إلا في عهده، ولا خرج إلى أهل الردة إلا أصحاب أبي بكر الله وعنهم فكانوا شاكرين وأبو بكر إمامهم، والله الموفق.
And the proof for it is His saying: 'Then if he dies or is killed, will you turn back on your heels? And whoever turns back on his heels will never harm God at all, and God will reward the thankful.' So He, glorified, informed that among them are those who turn back on their heels and among them are those who thank God by patiently adhering to His religion and upholding it. And al-Hasan said: 'Abu Bakr was the imam of the thankful, because those who patiently adhered to the religion of God were the ones who fought the apostates, and he was their imam.' And God is the grantor of success.

Commentary
This passage provides a second Qur'anic argument for Abu Bakr's caliphate. It cites Surah Al Imran (3:144), which addresses the hypothetical death or killing of the Prophet and warns against turning back on one's heels. The verse distinguishes between those who apostatize and those who are 'thankful'—meaning those who remain steadfast in faith. Al-Hasan al-Basri is quoted as saying that Abu Bakr was the 'imam of the thankful,' because the steadfast believers who fought the apostates were the thank‐ful ones, and Abu Bakr led them. The passage argues that the verse's reference to turning back at the Prophet's death was fulfilled in the apostasy wars during Abu Bakr's caliphate, since apostasy occurred only then. Moreover, those who fought the apostates were Abu Bakr's supporters, proving he was their leader. Thus, the verse implicitly validates Abu Bakr's leadership.

Consensus as a Decisive Proof for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Proof that consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate is decisive evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why consensus (ijma) is considered a decisive proof in Maturidi theology, specifically applied to Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Core Idea
Consensus carries the same certainty as the Qur'an and authentic Sunnah.

This lesson examines the third proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate presented by Imam Nasafi: the consensus of the Companions. The passage establishes consensus (ijma) as a definitive source of knowledge, not merely a probable one, and applies it to a historical creedal issue. Understanding this argument clarifies how Maturidi scholars integrated transmitted proofs (naqli) with rational ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the author's claim.
Notice the key terms: إجماع, حجة قاطعة.
2
Identify the claim
Find where the author states that consensus is a decisive proof. Note the phrase 'حجة قاطعة' and what it entails.
3
Examine the comparison
Notice how the author compares consensus to the Qur'an and the Prophet's reports/miracles. This shows the level of certainty attributed to consensus.
The comparison emphasizes that consensus yields certain knowledge, not just probability.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why consensus is presented as a proof for a creedal matter (caliphate), not just a legal issue. This highlights the theological weight of ijma in Maturidi thought.
The passage uses consensus to settle a historical and doctrinal dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على صحة خلافته إجماع الصحابة عليها، والإجماع حجة قاطعة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كنص كتاب الله تعالى وخبر الرسول عليه السلام المسموع منه أو الآيات الثابتة عنه بطريق لا يتوهم فيه شبهة الانقطاع.
And the proof for the validity of his caliphate is the consensus of the companions upon it. And consensus is a decisive proof that necessitates certain knowledge, like the text of the Book of God Almighty and the report of the Messenger, peace be upon him, heard from him, or the miracles established from him through a path in which no suspicion of discontinuity is imagined.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof: the consensus (ijma') of the companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate. It asserts that consensus is a definitive proof (hujja qati'a) that produces certain knowledge ('ilm qat'an), equating its authority to that of the Qur'anic text and the Prophet's reports or miracles that are transmitted without any possibility of break in the chain. The author emphasizes the binding nature of consensus as a source of theology and law, and applies it specifically to the question of Abu Bakr's legitimacy. The phrase 'the miracles established from him' likely refers to the Prophet's miracles (mu'jizat) that are similarly transmitted via continuous testimony.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجة قاطعة
خلافة
الصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is merely a logical inference or a probable proof, not equivalent to revelation.
Correction: The passage asserts that consensus is a decisive proof that produces certain knowledge, just like the Qur'an and authentic Sunnah. It is not a matter of probability but of certainty.

Checkpoint
How does the author classify the authority of consensus (ijma)?
A) It is a probable proof that requires interpretation
B) It is a decisive proof that produces certain knowledge, like the Qur'an and authentic Sunnah
C) It is a proof only for legal matters, not for creed
D) It is a proof that depends on the caliph's approval

Key Takeaway
Consensus (ijma) is not merely a legal convention but a divinely-guided source of certainty, comparable to the Qur'an and authentic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Companions unanimously agreed on Abu Bakr. Are you part of that consensus by your acceptance, or are you separate from the community of the Companions? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to the first generation?

Quranic Proof for the Caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar
Nasafi interprets a Quranic verse to support the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses a Quranic verse to establish the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar through a conditional interpretation and a separate proof from the concept of 'grateful ones'.

Core Idea
A single verse can validate both caliphates through two complementary interpretations.

In this passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla, Imam Nasafi offers a subtle scriptural argument for the legitimacy of the first two caliphs. He shows that the same Quranic verse can be read in two ways, each supporting one of the caliphs. The argument is both logical and historical, tying the verse's mention of 'turning back' to the apostasy wars under Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure and the use of 'if'.
The author presents two parallel conditional statements.
2
Identify the two conditional interpretations
Notice the two possibilities: if the verse refers to Banu Hanifa, it supports Abu Bakr's caliphate; if it refers to the Persians, it supports Umar's caliphate. Then see how each leads to the other caliph's legitimacy.
This dual interpretation is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the second proof from the 'grateful' ones
Read the second part where the author cites the verse and links 'the grateful' to those who fought apostasy. Note how Abu Bakr is identified as their imam, reinforcing his caliphate.
This proof is historical, connecting the verse to the Ridda wars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان المراد منه بني حنيفة فقد كان الداعي إليهم أبا بكر فثبتت بذلك خلافته، وإذا ثبتت خلافته ثبتت خلافة من عقد هو له الخلافة واستخلفه بعده وهو عمر، وإن كان المراد منه أهل فارس فالداعي إليهم كان عمر فثبتت خلافته، وبثبوت خلافته ثبتت خلافة من كان هو خليفة له عقد هو له وهو أبو بكر، فكان في الآية دليل خلافة الشيخين، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى: أَفَإِنْ مَاتَ أَوْ قُتِلَ انقَلَبْتُمْ عَلَى أَعْقَابِكُمْ وَمَن يَنقَلِبْ عَلَى عَقِبَيْهِ فَلَن يَضُرَّ اللَّهَ شَيْئًا وَسَيَجْزِي اللَّهُ الشَّاكِرِينَ ) فأخبر عز وجل أن فيهم من ينقلب على عقبيه وفيهم من يشكر الله بالصبر على دينه والقيام به وقال الحسن: كان أبو بكر إمام الشاكرين، لأن الذين صبروا على دين الله هم الذين حاربوا المنقلبين فكان هو إمامهم، وكان ذكر الانقلاب من الله تعالى عند موت نبيه عليه السلام، فثبت أنه كان في عهد أبي بكر إذ لا ارتداد كان إلا في عهده، ولا خرج إلى أهل الردة إلا أصحاب أبي بكر فكانوا شاكرين وأبو بكر إمامهم، والله الموفق.
If what is meant by it is Banu Hanifa, then the one who summoned them was Abu Bakr, so his caliphate is thereby established. And if his caliphate is established, then the caliphate of the one for whom he held the caliphate and appointed as successor after him—that is, Umar—is established. And if what is meant by it is the people of Persia, then the one who summoned them was Umar, so his caliphate is established. And by establishing his caliphate, the caliphate of the one who was his successor, whom he held it for, that is, Abu Bakr, is established. So in the verse there is evidence for the caliphate of the two shaykhs. The evidence for it is His statement, exalted: 'Then if he dies or is killed, will you turn back on your heels? And whoever turns back on his heels will never harm Allah at all, and Allah will reward the grateful.' So He, mighty and majestic, informed that among them are those who turn back on their heels and among them are those who thank Allah by patiently adhering to His religion and upholding it. And al-Hasan said: Abu Bakr was the imam of the grateful ones, because those who were patient upon the religion of Allah were the ones who fought the apostates, so he was their imam. And the mention of turning back from Allah, exalted, came at the death of His Prophet, peace be upon him, so it is established that it was during the era of Abu Bakr, since apostasy occurred only during his era, and none went forth to the apostates except the companions of Abu Bakr, so they were grateful and Abu Bakr was their imam. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents a rational and scriptural argument for the legitimacy of the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar. The author begins with a conditional analysis of the Quranic verse (Al Imran 3:144). He proposes two possible referents for those who 'turn back on their heels': if they are Banu Hanifa (the tribe of Musaylimah the false prophet), then it was Abu Bakr who summoned them to fight apostasy, thereby establishing his caliphate, and since Umar was appointed by Abu Bakr, his caliphate also is established. If the referent is the people of Persia, then it was Umar who summoned them, and since Umar was appointed by Abu Bakr, Abu Bakr's caliphate is likewise confirmed. Thus the verse implicitly validates both. The author then quotes the verse and notes that it mentions both 'those who turn back' and 'the grateful'. He cites Hasan al-Basri, who identified Abu Bakr as the imam of the grateful ones because those who steadfastly fought the apostates were the grateful, and Abu Bakr led them. He stresses that the verse's mention of turning back occurred at the Prophet's death, which historically matches the apostasy wars under Abu Bakr. The argument is cumulative: the verse's context and the historical fact of apostasy only during Abu Bakr's reign demonstrate his rightful caliphate, and by extension Umar's.

خلافة
الشيخين
انقلاب
الشاكرين
ارتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only refers to one group (either Banu Hanifa or Persians) and thus only supports one caliph.
Correction: The author shows that whichever interpretation is taken, it supports one caliph, and because their caliphates are linked (Abu Bakr appointed Umar), both are established.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Quranic verse 'if he dies or is killed...' support the caliphate of both Abu Bakr and Umar?
A) It explicitly names them as successors.
B) It can refer either to Banu Hanifa (fought by Abu Bakr) or to the Persians (fought by Umar), thus each interpretation validates one caliph.
C) It predicts their reigns through a prophecy.
D) It was revealed specifically to justify Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse implicitly confirms the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar through dual interpretation and the link to the grateful ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a crisis of faith or confusion, do you have the same resolve as the grateful ones who fought apostasy, or are you quick to turn back? What would it take for you to be among the 'shakireen'?
?
The verse was revealed at the death of the Prophet. Think about the moment when your own guiding figure or comfort is removed—do you rely on the same principles or do you waver? How can you prepare now to be steadfast?
?
Abu Bakr was the imam of the grateful ones because he led the fight against apostasy. Who in your life serves as a model of steadfastness? Are you willing to be that model for someone else?

Response to Objection of Ali's Delayed Allegiance
Nasafi answers the objection that Ali's delayed pledge invalidates consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi defends the consensus of the Companions by interpreting Ali's delayed pledge as a sign of careful deliberation and eventual acceptance of truth.

Core Idea
Ali's delay shows his careful deliberation, not dissent.

This passage addresses a key objection raised against the consensus of the Companions: that Ali ibn Abi Talib delayed his pledge to Abu Bakr by several months. Critics argue this invalidates the consensus. Nasafi responds by interpreting the delay as a sign of Ali’s prudence and commitment to truth, not opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the passage to see the structure: the opponent claims Ali's delay breaks consensus; Nasafi gives a multi-part response.
Notice the smooth transition from objection to refutation.
2
Identify the reasons for Ali's delay
Nasafi lists reasons: Ali was reflecting to discern the truth, and he would have opposed openly if wrong. His eventual agreement was based on truth.
Contrast this with the view that he delayed out of opposition.
3
Contrast with the Rawafid claim
Nasafi explicitly rejects the idea that Ali acted out of fear, citing his known bravery, strength of clan, and religious firmness.
This shows the sectarian dimension of the argument.
4
Consider the argument's strength
Reflect on how this defense reinforces consensus by using Ali's own character and eventual pledge as proof.
The passage uses biographical evidence to support theological claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: دعوى الإجماع ممنوعة فإن عليًا تأخر عن بيعته ستة أشهر أو أربعة أشهر. قلنا: وقد انعقد بعد بيعته وبه يحتج فلم يكن في هذا السؤال لهم منفعة، ثم تأخره محمول على الاشتغال بالنظر ليتضح له وجوه الصواب فيتابع عامة الصحابة ويبايعه، أو يبدو له وجه خطئهم فيعلن مخالفتهم، ويجاهر بالمكاشفة ويشهر عليهم سيفه، كما يليق بكمال علمه وقوة ديانته ورباطة جأشه، ولذا لم يظهر في تلك المدة والمنازعة، فلما لاح له بعد طول التردد وإدمان النظر وجه الصواب وافق غيره من الصحابة وبايع الصديق اتباعًا للحق كما هو اللائق بحاله في جلالة قدره وعظم شأنه، لا خوفا على نفسه وأهاليه وتوقيًا على مكروه يناله في نفسه كما بهم الروافض، إذ هو بعيد عن حاله ممتنع منه على ما تقدم من ذكره في بيان شهامته وصرامته ومشهور نجدته وشجاعته ومخبور غنائه وبسالته، وما عرف من صلابته في ديانته وقوة عشيرته وعزة قبيلته.
If they say: The claim of consensus is denied, for Ali delayed his pledge by six months or four months. We say: Consensus was concluded after his pledge, and it is used as a proof, so this question yields no benefit for them. Then his delay is attributed to his preoccupation with reflection to clarify the correct aspects for himself, so that he would follow the general body of Companions and pledge, or if he saw a flaw in their position he would declare his opposition, openly confront, and raise his sword against them, as befits his complete knowledge, strength of religion, and fortitude. Therefore, no strife or dispute appeared during that period. When, after prolonged hesitation and deep deliberation, the correct course became clear to him, he agreed with the other Companions and pledged to al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr), following the truth, as befits his exalted status and great dignity, not out of fear for himself or his family and to avoid harm that might befall him, as the Rawafid claim, for that is far from his character and impossible for him, given what has been mentioned about his bravery, sharpness, renowned courage, known valor, and his firmness in religion, strength of his clan, and honor of his tribe.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage follows an objection-and-answer format. The objection: the opponent denies the consensus because Ali delayed his bay'a for four to six months. The answer consists of several points: First, consensus was formed after Ali's pledge, so the delay does not negate the consensus. Second, Ali's delay is interpreted as a period of reflection (tawaqquf) to discern the truth; he either would agree with the majority or oppose openly—his character (knowledge, piety, fortitude) precludes passive dissent. Third, his eventual agreement was based on truth (haqq), not fear, as his well-known bravery and tribal strength remove any possibility of taqiyya. The author contrasts this with the Rawafid's claim of fear, calling it incompatible with Ali's known attributes. The argument thus defends the authority of consensus by showing that the sole apparent counterexample (Ali) actually reinforces it through eventual acceptance.

النظر
الشهامة
الشجاعة
الروافض
تقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ali's delay in pledging indicates he rejected Abu Bakr's caliphate.
Correction: The passage explains that Ali's delay was a period of reflection to discern the truth, and he eventually pledged based on that truth, confirming the consensus.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain Ali's delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr?
A) He was ill and unable to attend.
B) He was reflecting to determine the correct course, eventually agreeing based on truth.
C) He opposed Abu Bakr but was forced to pledge by the majority.
D) He was waiting for a sign from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Ali's delayed pledge does not break consensus; it confirms it through his eventual allegiance based on truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision where your initial instinct differs from the majority of trustworthy people around you, do you honestly seek the truth as Ali did, or do you stubbornly hold onto your position out of pride? What would it look like for you to submit to the truth once it becomes clear, even if it means changing your stance?
?
The Rawafid claimed Ali delayed out of fear—a slander that Nasafi refutes by highlighting Ali's bravery. Are there people in your life whose good faith you betray by assuming bad motives? How can you train your heart to think well of others, especially when their actions are ambiguous?
?
Ali's deliberation was a process of seeking truth, not a rejection. When you are faced with a teaching from the righteous predecessors that seems difficult, do you dismiss it outright or do you patiently study until the truth becomes clear, then act on it? What is one area where you need to follow this example today?

The Contradiction in the Rawafid Claim of Taqiyya
Nasafi refutes the Rawafid claim of taqiyya as contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Rawafid claim that Ali pledged to Abu Bakr out of taqiyya is internally contradictory.

Core Idea
Their own description of Ali and Abu Bakr destroys their claim of taqiyya.

Imam Nasafi, in this brief but powerful passage, points out a glaring logical inconsistency in the Rawafid position. Before we examine the text, consider: if someone claims that a strong, brave person acted out of fear of a weak, cowardly person, something is clearly wrong. Let's see how Nasafi exposes this contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own expression come through before analyzing.
Notice the word 'astonishing' (أعجب) — it indicates the author's strong reaction.
2
Identify the two descriptions
The Rawafid describe Abu Bakr as weak and cowardly, and Ali as strong and extremely courageous. Find these in the text.
These are their own claims, not necessarily historical facts.
3
Understand why taqiyya is illogical
Ask yourself: if Ali is as strong and brave as they claim, why would he need to dissimulate out of fear of a weak and cowardly person? The contradiction becomes clear.
This is the core argument of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أعجب الأشياء دعوى الروافض أن عليًا بايع أبا بكر تقية مع ما يدعون من وصف أبي بكر بالضعف والجبن ووصف علي بالقوة وغاية الشجاعة. والله الموفق.
And among the most astonishing things is the claim of the Rawafid that Ali pledged to Abu Bakr out of dissimulation (taqiyya), despite their description of Abu Bakr as weak and cowardly, and their description of Ali as strong and extremely courageous. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This is a brief refutation (radd) of a specific objection. The Rawafid (a pejorative term for Shia who reject the first three caliphs) claim that Ali pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr only out of taqiyya (dissimulation). The author points out an internal contradiction: the Rawafid themselves describe Abu Bakr as weak and cowardly, and Ali as strong and extremely courageous. If that were true, there would be no need for Ali to fear Abu Bakr or to pretend. The claim is therefore 'astonishing' (a'jab al-ashya'). The statement ends with a customary prayer, 'Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
الروافض
تقية
ضعف
جبن
شجاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqiyya is a valid explanation for Ali's pledge because he might have feared Abu Bakr despite being stronger.
Correction: The Rawafid's own premises (Abu Bakr's weakness and cowardice, Ali's strength and courage) make such fear impossible, so taqiyya is logically inconsistent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author find the Rawafid's claim that Ali pledged under taqiyya to be 'astonishing'?
A) Because Ali was actually weaker than Abu Bakr.
B) Because they simultaneously say Abu Bakr is weak and cowardly while Ali is strong and brave, making taqiyya unnecessary.
C) Because taqiyya is forbidden in Islam.
D) Because the claim is not found in any historical source.

Key Takeaway
The Rawafid claim of taqiyya is self-contradictory based on their own characterizations of Abu Bakr and Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that conflicts with evidence, do you check for internal contradictions? Can you recall a time you accepted an explanation without testing its logical consistency?
?
What would it take for you to admit that a cherished belief is self-contradictory? Are you willing to examine your own convictions with the same scrutiny Nasafi applies here?
?
How often do you use intellectual shortcuts like 'they did it out of fear' to justify actions without examining the actual dynamics? Will you commit to asking 'does this explanation really make sense?' before accepting it?

Refuting the Objection on Ali's Delay in Allegiance
Refutation of an objection regarding Ali's allegiance delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Ali's delayed allegiance does not invalidate the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate, and how the author defends Ali's integrity.

Core Idea
Ali's delay in allegiance was due to reflection, not rejection.

A common objection raised against the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate is that Imam Ali delayed his allegiance for several months. In this passage, Imam Nasafi refutes this objection by first affirming that consensus was already established after Ali's allegiance, and then providing a noble explanation for the delay: Ali was engaged in careful investigation to determine the truth. This lesson examines the argument and defends both the consensus of the Companions and Ali's character.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objection stated at the start of the passage: the opponents claim consensus is impossible because Ali delayed his allegiance.
Notice the precise wording: 'دعوى الإجماع ممنوعة فإن عليًا تأخر عن بيعته'
2
Examine the first response
The author first responds that the consensus was concluded after Ali's allegiance, so the objection has no benefit. Understand that later acceptance does not invalidate earlier consensus.
3
Analyze the explanation for the delay
The author provides a two-part explanation: Ali was occupied with reflection to determine the truth, and if he had found error, he would have openly opposed. After reflection, he agreed with the majority.
This interpretation is consistent with Ali's known character of bravery and truth-seeking.
4
Reject the Rafidah claim
Finally, note how the author rejects the Rafidah claim that Ali acted out of fear (taqiyya), citing his well-known bravery.
This underscores the author's commitment to defending the integrity of the early community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: دعوى الإجماع ممنوعة فإن عليًا تأخر عن بيعته ستة أشهر أو أربعة أشهر. قلنا: وقد انعقد بعد بيعته وبه يحتج فلم يكن في هذا السؤال لهم منفعة، ثم تأخره محمول على الاشتغال بالنظر ليتضح له وجوه الصواب فيتابع عامة الصحابة ويبايعه، أو يبدو له وجه خطئهم فيعلن مخالفتهم، ويجاهر بالمكاشفة ويشهر عليهم سيفه، كما يليق بكمال علمه وقوة ديانته ورباطة جأشه، ولذا لم يظهر في تلك المدة المنازعة، فلما لاح له بعد طول التردد وإدمان النظر وجه الصواب وافق غيره من الصحابة وبايع الصديق اتباعًا للحق كما هو اللائق بحاله في جلالة قدره وعظم شأنه، لا خوفا على نفسه وأهاليه وتوقيًا على مكروه يناله في نفسه كما يزعم الروافض، إذ هو بعيد عن حاله ممتنع منه على ما تقدم من ذكره في بيان شهامته وصرامته ومشهور نجدته وشجاعته ومخبور غنائه وبسالته.
If they say: The claim of consensus is denied, for Ali delayed in his allegiance by six or four months. We say: The consensus was concluded after his allegiance, and it is that which is adduced as proof, so there is no benefit in this question for them. Then his delay is attributed to being occupied with reflection so that the aspects of correctness become clear to him, so that he then follows the generality of the Companions and gives allegiance, or that the aspect of their error becomes apparent to him so that he declares his opposition, proclaims openly, and unsheathes his sword against them, as befits his perfect knowledge, strength of religion, and steadfastness of heart. Thus he did not display dispute during that period. When, after lengthy deliberation and persistent reflection, the aspect of correctness became clear to him, he agreed with the other Companions and gave allegiance to al-Siddiq, following the truth, as is fitting for his station in the majesty of his rank and greatness of his status, not out of fear for himself and his family, nor out of caution against harm that might befall him, as the Rafidah claim. For that is far from his state and impossible from him, based on what has preceded of his mention in the explanation of his bravery, resoluteness, his well-known valor, courage, his abundant exploits, and his prowess.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by opponents (likely the Rawafid/Shia) against the validity of the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate. They argued that consensus cannot be claimed because Imam Ali delayed his allegiance for several months. The author (Imam Nasafi) responds by first pointing out that the consensus was already established after Ali's allegiance, and his delay does not negate that. He then provides a charitable interpretation of the delay: Ali was engaged in careful investigation (ijtihad) to determine the truth. If he had found the majority in error, he would have openly opposed them, as befits his courage and religious commitment. But after reflection, he recognized the correctness of the community's choice and gave allegiance willingly, not out of fear. The author rejects the Rafidi claim of taqiyya (dissimulation), emphasizing that Ali's known bravery and strength make such fear-based behavior impossible. The passage defends the integrity of the early community and the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Key Terms
الإجماع
البيعة
التقية
الصديق
الروافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's delay in giving allegiance to Abu Bakr indicates that he rejected Abu Bakr's caliphate and only acquiesced under duress.
Correction: The author explains that Ali's delay was due to careful reflection to determine the truth, and he eventually gave allegiance willingly, consistent with his character. The claim of duress (taqiyya) is incompatible with his known bravery.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the reason for Ali's delay in giving allegiance to Abu Bakr?
A) He was forced by the Rafidah to wait.
B) He was engaged in reflection to determine the truth.
C) He was afraid of harm to himself and his family.
D) He did not accept Abu Bakr as caliph.

Key Takeaway
Ali's delayed allegiance does not undermine the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate; it was a period of legitimate reflection ending in willing acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision where the majority of sincere believers seem to be on one side, do you pause to reflect and seek the truth like Ali, or do you immediately follow your own preferences? What would it take for you to submit to the truth even if it means setting aside your initial hesitation?
?
The Rafidah accused Ali of acting out of fear. Are there people in your life whose character you have misjudged by attributing base motives to their actions? How can you train yourself to assume the best of others, especially those known for their piety and courage?
?
Ali eventually gave allegiance after long deliberation, accepting the consensus. Is there a matter of religious truth that you have been resisting, perhaps due to pride or fear of change? What concrete step will you take today to align yourself with the truth, even if it requires humility?

Refutation of the Rafidah Claim of Taqiyya
Author ridicules the Rafidah's contradictory claim about taqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency in the Rafidah's claim that Ali gave allegiance to Abu Bakr out of taqiyya.

Core Idea
If Ali was brave and Abu Bakr weak, taqiyya makes no sense.

The author begins by ridiculing a contradictory claim from the Rafidah sect: they say Ali gave allegiance out of fear, yet they also describe Ali as extremely brave and Abu Bakr as weak. This introduction sets the stage for a broader argument about consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author's tone of astonishment.
2
Identify the contradiction
The Rafidah claim that Ali acted out of taqiyya contradicts their own descriptions of both men. Ask yourself: if Ali was so brave, why would he fear a weak man?
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author finds this claim 'among the most astonishing things.' What does this reveal about the Rafidah's inconsistency?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أعجب الأشياء دعوى الروافض أن عليًا بايع أبا بكر تقية مع ما يدعون من وصف أبي بكر بالضعف والجبن ووصف علي بالقوة وغاية الشجاعة والله الموفق. الإجماع حجة ملزمة كالنص: فلو أنهم مالوا إلى إنكار كون الإجماع حجة فنثبت عليهم بالدلائل المشهورة التي أوردها أئمة الدين في إثبات كون الإجماع حجة، لا معنى لاشتغالنا في هذا الكتاب بذلك لشهرتها في كل كتاب صنف في أصول الفقه، ومن أراد الوقوف عليها لا يعدمها ولا يتعذر عليه الظفر بها. على أن رأي علي وقوله عندهم حجة، وقد ثبت بالنقل المتواتر الذي ينسب جاحده إلى العناد والمكابرة بيعته أبا بكر واعترافه بخلافته، فيكون قوله حجة كافية لصحة خلافته، ولا يقع لهم فيما يزعمون أن الإجماع مع مخالفة البعض غير منعقد لأن خلاف البعض لو توهم فعلی به ثبت وفاقه وقوله عندكم حجة كافية، ثم نقول لو وجد خلاف البعض لظهر إذ لا يتوهم في العادات الجارية خلاف من يعد بخلافه خلافا في مثل هذه الحادثة ثم تتكتم ولا تشتهر، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يزعم ملحد أن القرآن لعله عورض ولم ينقل معارضة، وكذا معجزة كل نبي فيصير ذلك طريقًا لإبطال الرسالات وتعطيل المعجزات والآيات التي أتى بها الرسل عليهم السلام، وذا كفر وباطل فكذا ما أدى إليه، بل فيه إنكار الشاهدات وجحد الضروريات وإفساد الألسنة واللغات، إذ ما من كلام يتكلم به إلا ويمكن لقائل أن يقول إن ما ذكرت من الألفاظ ليست بدلالة على ما قصدت إليه من المعاني إذ لم يثبت ذلك باتفاق أهل اللسان أو مساعدة أهل البلدة أو القبيلة إذ يتوهم أن بعضهم يجعل هذه الألفاظ عبارات عن أضداد هذه المعاني، وما هذا سبيله لا يجوز التفات إليه ولا الاشتغال بجواب من جعله معلولاً عليه لتأييد نحلته وتقوية رأيه ومذهبه.
Among the most astonishing things is the claim of the Rafidah that Ali gave allegiance to Abu Bakr out of taqiyya (dissimulation), despite what they claim in describing Abu Bakr as weak and cowardly and describing Ali as strong and extremely courageous. And Allah is the grantor of success. Consensus is a binding proof like the text: If they incline to deny that consensus is proof, we will establish it against them by the well-known proofs that the imams of the religion have mentioned in establishing that consensus is proof; there is no point in our occupying ourselves with that in this book due to its fame in every book written in usul al-fiqh. Whoever wishes to find them will not lack them and will not be unable to obtain them. Moreover, the opinion and statement of Ali is, according to them, proof, and it has been established by mutawatir transmission—whose denier is attributed to stubbornness and obstinacy—that he gave allegiance to Abu Bakr and acknowledged his caliphate. Thus his statement is sufficient proof for the soundness of his caliphate, and they cannot claim that consensus with the opposition of some is not concluded, for if the opposition of some were imagined, then his (Ali's) agreement is established through it, and his statement is, according to you, sufficient proof. Then we say: if the opposition of some existed, it would have appeared, for it is not imagined in normal custom that the opposition of one who would be considered an opposition in such an event would then be concealed and not become famous. If this were permissible, then it would be permissible for an atheist to claim that the Qur'an was perhaps challenged and the challenge was not transmitted, and likewise for every prophet's miracle, making this a way to invalidate prophethood and nullify the miracles and signs brought by the messengers—peace be upon them—and that is disbelief and falsehood, and the same applies to what leads to it. Indeed, it involves denying self-evident things, repudiating necessary matters, and corrupting languages and tongues, for there is no speech uttered but that a speaker can say that the words you mentioned do not indicate the meanings you intend, since that is not established by the agreement of the people of the language or the consensus of the people of the town or tribe, imagining that some of them make these words expressions for the opposites of these meanings. What is of this nature should not be paid attention to, nor should one occupy oneself with answering one who makes it an objection to support his sect and strengthen his opinion and school.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a composite argument comprising two main parts. First, the author criticizes the Rafidah's contradictory stance: they claim Ali gave allegiance out of fear (taqiyya) while also describing Abu Bakr as weak and Ali as brave—if Ali was so brave, why would he fear a weak man? This ridicules their position. Second, he argues that consensus (ijma') is a binding proof and that even if some thought opponents existed, Ali's own allegiance suffices to establish the consensus because his view is considered authoritative by the Rafidah themselves. He then argues that if an opponent's dissent existed, it would have become known due to historical prominence; otherwise, it could lead to the absurd denial of all transmitted knowledge, such as the Qur'an and miracles. The author emphasizes that rejecting consensus on such speculative grounds would undermine all language and communication. Therefore, the objection is invalid and not worth addressing in detail.

Key Terms
التقية (al-taqiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ali gave allegiance out of fear (taqiyya) due to Abu Bakr's power.
Correction: The author points out that this is inconsistent because they themselves describe Abu Bakr as weak and Ali as brave, so fear would be irrational.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author point out in the Rafidah's claim?
A) They say Ali was brave and Abu Bakr weak, yet claim Ali acted out of fear.
B) They deny the caliphate of Abu Bakr entirely.
C) They claim consensus is not binding.
D) They reject the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The Rafidah's claim about taqiyya is self-contradictory given their own descriptions of the two figures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your own life where you hold two beliefs that logically contradict each other? What would it take for you to expose that inconsistency today?
?
Have you ever justified a position you knew deep down was contradictory? How did that affect your integrity? Will you commit to examining one such contradiction this week?
?
The author uses astonishment to highlight absurdity. Is there a belief you hold that would seem astonishingly contradictory if laid bare? Are you willing to re-examine it now?

The Binding Nature of Consensus and Refutation of Hidden Opposition
Author establishes consensus as binding proof and refutes hidden dissent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why consensus is a binding proof and why the possibility of hidden opposition is invalid.

Core Idea
Consensus is binding because key figures like Ali agreed, and hidden dissent would unravel all knowledge.

After ridiculing the taqiyya claim, the author turns to the positive proof: consensus (ijma') is a binding proof like the text. He argues that even if some claimed there was opposition, Ali's own allegiance suffices to establish consensus, and if opposition existed it would have become famous. He then warns that accepting concealed opposition would lead to denying the Qur'an and miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the entire passage on consensus, from 'الإجماع حجة ملزمة' to the end.
2
Identify the key argument
The author says consensus is like the text (binding). He then uses Ali's allegiance as evidence that consensus was achieved, and that any imagined opposition would have been known historically.
3
Consider the slippery slope
The author argues that if concealed opposition were possible, it would lead to absurd outcomes like denying the Qur'an's challenge and prophets' miracles. Why is this a powerful refutation?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by saying such objections are not worth answering because they undermine all language. How does this solidify the binding nature of consensus?

?

?

How can you cultivate a heart that embraces all of Allah's proofs equally?

The Shia Argument from the Verse of Wilayah
Shia objection using Quran 5:55 to argue for Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia interpretation of Quran 5:55 as a proof for Ali's caliphate and the basis of their argument.

Core Idea
Shia argue that Quran 5:55 designates Ali as the political successor (wali) after the Prophet.

In this lesson, we examine a key Shia proof text: the verse of wilayah (Quran 5:55). The Shia interpret this verse as referring specifically to Imam Ali when he gave charity while bowing in prayer. They conclude that the term 'wali' implies political authority, making Ali the rightful successor. Imam Nasafi presents their argument before beginning his refutation. This lesson focuses on understanding the claim itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains the Shia quoting Quran 5:55 and then making their inference. Notice how they connect the verse to Ali.
The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the core claim is complete.
2
Identify the key proof
Identify the two parts: the verse they cite and the inference they draw. The verse mentions 'those who give zakah while bowing' — Shia tafsir links this to Ali. They then claim that 'wali' means political leader.
3
Consider the term 'wali'
Reflect on the Arabic word 'wali' (ولي). It can mean friend, ally, guardian, or ruler. The Shia interpret it here as 'successor' or 'ruler'. This is crucial for the debate.
The author's refutation (not given here) likely challenges this interpretation.
4
Prepare for the refutation
Understanding the argument fully prepares you to follow the author's response in subsequent lessons. Note that the page ends abruptly; the refutation is continued elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا أن الله تعالى قال: إِنَّمَا وَلِيُّكُمُ اللهُ وَرَسُولُهُ وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا الَّذِينَ يُقِيمُونَ الصَّلوةَ وَيُؤْتُونَ الزَّكوةَ وَهُمْ رَكِعُونَ قالوا: وهو علي على الله فوجب أن يكون وليا بعد الرسول عليه السلام، وإنما
If they say that Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your ally (wali) is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe, who establish prayer and give zakah and bow down.' They say: And he is 'Ali—may Allah be pleased with him—so it is necessary that he be the wali after the Messenger, peace be upon him. And
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a refutation against a Shia argument. The opponents quote Quran 5:55, which mentions 'those who believe, who establish prayer and give zakah while bowing.' According to Shia tafsir, this verse was revealed regarding Imam Ali when he gave a ring in charity while bowing in prayer. They claim that the term 'wali' in the verse implies political authority (wilayah) and thus Ali should be the successor (wali) after the Prophet. The author (Imam Nasafi) presents their claim as the beginning of a response, but the text cuts off at the page boundary. The full response would likely include a refutation based on the meaning of 'wali' and the context of the verse. The lesson presents the objection verbatim to be answered in following pages.

الولاية (al-wilayah)
الآية (al-ayah)
الراكعون (al-rak'un)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Quran 5:55 explicitly designates Ali as the political ruler after the Prophet.
Correction: The Shia interpretation relies on specific assumptions about the verse's occasion of revelation and the meaning of 'wali'. The author will challenge both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Consensus Argument Against Hidden Dissent
Refutation of hidden dissent as undermining consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hidden dissent cannot be accepted as a valid objection against consensus, and how the same reasoning protects the integrity of all transmitted knowledge.

Core Idea
If hidden dissent were possible, all transmitted knowledge would be suspect.

This passage addresses a key objection against the consensus (ijma) of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate. The author shows that the objection is self-defeating: if we accept the possibility of hidden dissent, then we must also accept absurd consequences that undermine the Qur'an, miracles, and even language itself. It is a masterful demonstration of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument: it starts with a concrete historical fact and expands into a logical reduction.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection and the response
The objection is that consensus might be invalid because some unknown person dissented. The author responds by pointing out that if that dissent existed, it would have become famous, and even then, the dissenter's own agreement would be evidence.
3
Understand the analogy with revelation
The author draws an analogy: if hidden dissent is allowed, an atheist could claim that the Qur'an was secretly challenged, or a prophet's miracle was secretly countered. This would destroy the basis of prophethood.
This is the key move: showing the argument leads to absurdity.
4
See the extension to language
The argument is extended: the same logic would allow anyone to deny that words mean what they conventionally mean, corrupting all language. Therefore, such objections are not worth entertaining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن رأي عليه وقوله عندهم حجة، وقد ثبت بالنقل المتواتر الذي ينسب جاحده إلى العناد والمكابرة بيعته أبا بكر واعترافه بخلافته، فيكون قوله حجة كافية لصحة خلافته، ولا يقع لهم فيما يزعمون أن الإجماع مع مخالفة البعض غير منعقد (ويوهم أن بعضا خالف ولم يعلم يقينا عدم مخالفة البعض) لأن خلاف البعض لو توهم فعلى به ثبت وفاقه وقوله عندكم حجة كافية، ثم نقول لو وجد خلاف البعض لظهر إذ لا يتوهم في العادات الجارية خلاف من يعد بخلافه خلافا في مثل هذه الحادثة ثم تتكتم ولا تشتهر، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يزعم ملحد أن القرآن لعله عورض ولم ينقل معارضة، وكذا معجزة كل نبي فيصير ذلك طريقًا لإبطال الرسالات وتعطيل المعجزات والآيات التي أتى بها الرسل عليهم السلام، وذا كفر وباطل فكذا ما أدى إليه، بل فيه إنكار الشاهدات وجحد الضروريات وإفساد الألسنة واللغات، إذ ما من كلام يتكلم به إلا ويمكن لقائل أن يقول إن ما ذكرت من الألفاظ ليست بدلالة على ما قصدت إليه من المعاني إذ لم يثبت ذلك باتفاق أهل اللسان أو مساعدة أهل البلدة أو القبيلة إذ يتوهم أن بعضهم يجعل هذه الألفاظ عبارات عن أضداد هذه المعاني، وما هذا سبيله لا يجوز التفات إليه ولا الاشتغال بجواب من جعله معلولاً عليه لتأييد نحلته وتقوية رأيه ومذهبه.
That the opinion of Ali and his statement are a proof according to them, and it has been established by mutawatir transmission, the denier of which is attributed to stubbornness and obstinacy, that he pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr and acknowledged his caliphate, so his statement is a sufficient proof for the validity of his caliphate. And it does not occur to them in what they claim that consensus with the dissent of some is not concluded (and it is imagined that some dissented while it is not known with certainty that there was no dissent of some) because if dissent of some were imagined, then by it his agreement and statement would be established as a sufficient proof according to you. Then we say: if dissent of some existed, it would have appeared, because it is not imagined in the usual customs that dissent of one who is considered a dissenter would be hidden in such an incident and then concealed and not become famous. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for an atheist to claim that the Qur'an might have been challenged and the challenge not transmitted, and likewise the miracle of every prophet, making that a way to invalidate missions and nullify the miracles and signs brought by the messengers, peace be upon them. And that is unbelief and falsehood, so likewise what leads to it. Rather, in it is denial of witnessed things, rejection of necessities, and corruption of tongues and languages, because there is no speech spoken except that a speaker could say that what you mentioned of words is not an indication of the meanings you intended, since that has not been established by the agreement of the people of the language or the assistance of the people of the town or tribe, imagining that some of them make these words expressions for the opposites of these meanings. And what is like this, it is not permissible to pay attention to it nor to occupy oneself with answering one who makes it a defect to support his sect and strengthen his opinion and school.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphacy. The author argues that Ali's own opinion and statement are considered authoritative by the Shia (the opponents), and it is historically proven via mutawatir transmission that Ali pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr and acknowledged his caliphate. Therefore, his statement is sufficient proof for the validity of the caliphacy. The author then addresses the claim that consensus is invalid if there is a dissenter. He says that even if one imagines a dissenter, that dissenter's agreement is established by their own principles, because they consider the dissenter's statement as evidence. Moreover, if a real dissent existed, it would have become widely known, as it is impossible in normal circumstances for a significant dissent in such a major event to be hidden. The author then uses a reductio ad absurdum: if one allows the possibility of hidden dissent, then an atheist could claim that the Qur'an was actually challenged but the challenge was hidden, or that every prophet's miracle was counterfeited but not transmitted. This would destroy the basis of prophethood and revealed knowledge, which is absurd. He further argues that accepting such reasoning would lead to the denial of all empirical and linguistic certainties, since one could always claim that words mean something else. Therefore, such objections are not worthy of attention or response.

Key Terms
الإجماع
النقل المتواتر
الخلاف
الشاهدات
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the validity of consensus can be broken by the mere possibility of an unknown dissenter.
Correction: The author shows that such a possibility is not only historically unlikely but leads to destructive skepticism about all transmitted knowledge, including revelation and language.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would allowing the possibility of hidden dissent in consensus lead to absurd consequences?
A) It would imply that Ali's pledge was invalid.
B) It would allow atheists to claim that the Qur'an was secretly challenged.
C) It would mean that consensus requires unanimity in every age.
D) It would contradict the principle of mutawatir transmission.

Key Takeaway
Hidden dissent cannot break consensus because the same logic would unravel all transmitted knowledge, from revelation to language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself doubting an established truth of Islam because of an unsubstantiated possibility, do you realize that your doubt, if consistently applied, would also undermine the very sources of that truth? This week, identify one such doubt and trace its logical consequences: would it force you to reject other certainties you hold? Commit to silencing that doubt by recognizing its absurdity.
?
Is there an area of your life—perhaps in your relationships, your work, or your worship—where you have allowed a mere hypothetical objection to weaken your commitment to something you know to be true? What would it cost you to act on that certainty instead of entertaining the possibility? Today, choose one action that affirms the truth despite the lingering 'what if'.
?
The author argues that rejecting consensus based on mere possibility corrupts language itself. Reflect on a time when you used a skeptical argument to avoid a clear obligation. How did that affect your spiritual state? Repent from that mindset and resolve to base your actions on what is firmly established, not on idle possibilities.
