Refuting the Rafidah's Verse Argument for Ali's Imamate
Author refutes the Rafidah's verse-based proof for Ali's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'wali' in the Qur'anic verse does not prove Ali's immediate succession to the Prophet.

Core Idea
The term 'wali' does not automatically mean political leader.

The Rafidah (Shia) argue that the verse 'Your ally is only Allah, His Messenger, and those who believe...' refers to Ali, thus he must be the wali (guardian) after the Prophet. The author replies with multiple arguments dismantling this interpretation. This lesson walks through those refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation to understand the Rafidah's claim and the author's initial response.
The verse referenced is 5:55, though the passage does not quote it fully.
2
Identify the three refutations
The author gives three main points: (1) If the verse meant Ali, the Companions and Ali himself would not have acted as they did. (2) The verse is not explicit proof. (3) Even if it refers to Ali, 'wali' does not necessarily mean imam.
The third point is key: wali can mean friend or patron.
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this refutation is important for Sunni theology: it defends the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate and the consensus of the Companions.
The author notes that Ali himself pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا أن الله تعالى قال: إِنَّمَا وَلِيُّكُمُ اللهُ وَرَسُولُهُ وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا الَّذِينَ يُقِيمُونَ الصَّلوةَ () () قالوا: وهو على الله فوجب أن يكون وليا بعد الرسول عليه السلام، وإنما يكون وليًا أن لو كان متوليًا للإمامة فدل أنه الخليفة بعد الرسول عليه السلام. قلنا: لو كانت الآية منصرفة إلى ما قلتم لما خفي ذلك على الصحابة أولاً وعلى علي ثانيًا، ولما أجمعوا على خلافة غيره ولا بايع هو بنفسه غيره، ثم نقول: الآية بلا دليل، ولو جاز لهم حملها على علي له الله الجاز لغيرهم حملها على غيره، ولو سلم أن المراد به علي لم يلزم بإطلاق اسم الولي أن يكون إماما لأن الولي اسم يستعمل في الموالي، وذلك لا يستحيل في وصفه ولا يثبت به إمامته زمان أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما . ولا حجة لهم فيما يحتجون به من قولهم عليه السلام: "اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه" لأن الحديث ليس فيه دليل استخلافه بل دعاء له وإظهار لفضيلته. يحققه إن كان من يواليه الله تعالى لا يكون إمامًا، فإن قالوا: إنه عليه السلام قال: عاد من عاداه وأبو بكر عادى عليا فيكون الله تعالى معاديًا له بقضية هذا الحديث. قلنا: إن أبا بكر لم يعاد عليا ولا غصب حقا له، ولو كان فعل ذلك لما اجتمعت معه الصحابة ولا بايعه علي له، فهذا من الروافض طعن على خيار الناس وصدور الأمة ونقله الدين باتباعهم من عاداه الله تعالى بدعاء رسوله، فطعن على علي وأتباعه من صار بمعاداته عدوا الله تعالى ثم قد ثبت عن علي أنه قال: حدثني أبو بكر، وصدق أبو بكر، وروي عن علي الله عنه أنه قال : قال النبي عليه السلام لأبي
For they say that Allah Almighty said: 'Your ally is only Allah and His Messenger and those who believe, those who establish prayer...' They said: And he is Ali, so it is necessary that he be the guardian after the Messenger, peace be upon him, and he would only be a guardian if he were the one undertaking the imamate, so it indicates that he is the caliph after the Messenger, peace be upon him. We said: If the verse were directed to what you said, that would not be hidden from the Companions first nor from Ali second, and they would not have agreed on the caliphate of another, nor would he himself have pledged allegiance to another. Then we say: The verse is without proof, and if it were permissible for them to apply it to Ali, then it would be permissible for others to apply it to someone else. And if we concede that the intended is Ali, it does not necessarily follow from the mere name 'wali' that he is an imam, because the name wali is used for a patron, and that is not impossible as a description, and it does not establish his imamate during the time of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with them both. And they have no proof in what they argue from his statement, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him,' because the hadith does not contain evidence of his appointment as caliph, but rather a supplication for him and a manifestation of his virtue. This is verified because one whom Allah befriends is not necessarily an imam. If they say: He, peace be upon him, said: 'Be hostile to whoever is hostile to him,' and Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, so Allah would be hostile to him by the implication of this hadith. We say: Abu Bakr was not hostile to Ali, nor did he usurp any right of his, and if he had done that, the Companions would not have gathered with him, nor would Ali have pledged allegiance to him. This is a slander by the Rafidah against the best of people and the leaders of the community, and those who transmitted the religion, by claiming that they followed one whom Allah is hostile to due to the supplication of His Messenger. So they slander Ali and his followers, who by his enmity become enemies of Allah. Then it is established from Ali that he said: 'Abu Bakr narrated to me, and Abu Bakr spoke the truth,' and it is narrated from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: The Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Abu...
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two Rafidah (Shia) arguments for Ali's immediate succession. First, they cite the verse (5:55) and claim it refers to Ali, so he must be the wali (guardian/custodian) after the Prophet. The author responds with multiple points: (1) If the verse meant that, it would not have been hidden from the Companions and from Ali himself, and they would not have agreed on the caliphate of Abu Bakr nor would Ali have pledged allegiance to him. (2) The verse is not explicit (it is without proof). (3) Even if it refers to Ali, the term wali does not necessarily mean imam; it can mean patron or friend. (4) It does not establish his imamate during Abu Bakr's time. Second, they cite the hadith 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him...' The author says this is a supplication, not a designation of caliphate. Further, if they claim that Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, the author refutes that by noting that Ali himself pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr and narrated from him, proving there was no hostility. The passage ends abruptly while quoting another hadith from Ali.

Key Terms
الولي
الإمامة
الخليفة
الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'wali' in the Qur'an always implies political leadership and imamate.
Correction: The author explains that 'wali' can mean friend or patron, and it does not automatically designate someone as a caliph.

Checkpoint
What is one reason the author gives to refute the Rafidah's claim that the verse proves Ali's imamate?
A) The verse is abrogated
B) The verse is not explicit and could apply to others
C) The verse was only about the Prophet
D) The verse was revealed before Islam

Key Takeaway
The verse does not prove Ali's imamate because the term 'wali' is ambiguous and the Companions' consensus contradicts the Rafidah's interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that seems to support a beloved figure, do you ever stop to consider that the early community might have understood it differently? What would it take for you to trust their consensus over your own reading?
?
If you were in the position of Ali and the Companions, and you saw a verse that others interpreted as supporting your leadership, would you have the humility to recognize that the term 'wali' might not mean what you hope? How can you cultivate that humility in your own understanding of the Quran today?
?
The author argues that the verse is 'without evidence' (بلا دليل) for the Rafidah's claim. Are there any beliefs you hold that you have not checked against the clear understanding of the righteous predecessors? What specific step will you take this week to verify one such belief with a reliable scholar or text?

Refuting the Hadith Argument for Ali's Imamate
Author refutes the Rafidah's hadith-based proof for Ali's imamate and defends Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him' does not prove Ali's appointment as caliph.

Core Idea
The hadith is a supplication, not a political appointment.

The Rafidah also cite the famous hadith 'اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه' as proof that Ali should be the caliph. The author responds by clarifying the nature of the hadith and defending Abu Bakr’s relationship with Ali. This lesson examines the refutation and the additional defense against the accusation of Abu Bakr's hostility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the hadith
Read the Arabic and translation for the section beginning 'ولا حجة لهم...'. This contains the author's response to the hadith argument.
The hadith is a well-known supplication made by the Prophet regarding Ali.
2
Understand the two parts of the refutation
First, the author says the hadith is a supplication for Ali's virtue, not a designation of caliphate. Second, he refutes the implication that Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali.
The author uses historical evidence: Ali pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr and narrated from him.
3
Consider the polemical context
The passage defends the integrity of the Companions, especially Abu Bakr and Ali, and refutes the Rafidah's slander.
The author calls their claim a slander against the best of people.

Refuting the Verse of Wilayah
Imam Nasafi refutes the Shi'a argument from the verse of wilayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Shi'a claim that the verse of wilayah proves Ali's immediate caliphate after the Prophet.

Core Idea
One verse cannot override the consensus of the Companions.

The Shi'a cite verse 5:55 ('Your wali is only Allah, His Messenger, and those who believe...') as evidence for Ali's imamate. Imam Nasafi responds by pointing to the consensus of the Companions and the ambiguity of the term 'wali'. This lesson walks through his four counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shi'a claim
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage, which presents the Shi'a argument: the verse makes Ali a 'wali', and if he is in charge of the imamate, it indicates he is the caliph.
2
First argument: Consensus and Ali's actions
Nasafi argues that if the verse meant what the Shi'a claim, it would not have been hidden from the Companions, and they would not have agreed on the caliphate of Abu Bakr. Moreover, Ali himself gave allegiance to Abu Bakr.
This argument relies on the historical fact of consensus and Ali's own behavior.
3
Second argument: The verse is ambiguous
Nasafi says the verse is 'without proof' (ambiguous). If the Shi'a can interpret it as referring to Ali, others can interpret it differently. This undermines the claim of clear textual evidence.
4
Third argument: 'Wali' does not mean imam
Even if the verse refers to Ali, the word 'wali' commonly means friend or supporter, not necessarily imam. So the verse does not establish his imamate during Abu Bakr's time.
This is the key linguistic point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون وليًا أن لو كان متوليًا للإمامة فدل أنه الخليفة بعد الرسول (۱) عليه السلام. قلنا: لو كانت الآية منصرفة إلى ما قلتم لما خفي ذلك على الصحابة أولاً وعلى علي ثانيًا، ولما أجمعوا على خلافة غيره ولا بايع هو بنفسه غيره، ثم نقول: الآية بلا دليل، ولو جاز لهم حملها على علي له[؟] الجاز لغيرهم حملها على غيره، ولو سلم أن المراد به علي لم يلزم بإطلاق اسم الولي أن يكون إماما لأن الولي اسم يستعمل في الموالي، وذلك لا يستحيل في وصفه ولا يثبت به إمامته زمان أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما.
He would be a wali if he were in charge of the imamate, so it indicates that he is the caliph after the Messenger (peace be upon him). We said: If the verse were directed to what you say, it would not have been hidden from the Companions first and from Ali second, and they would not have agreed upon the caliphate of someone else, nor would he himself have given allegiance to someone else. Then we say: The verse is without proof, and if it were permissible for them to interpret it as referring to Ali, it would be permissible for others to interpret it as referring to someone else. And even if it is granted that the intended meaning is Ali, the absolute term 'wali' does not necessitate that he be an imam, because 'wali' is a name used for a friend (mawali), and that is not impossible in his description, nor does it establish his imamate during the time of Abu Bakr (may God be pleased with them both).
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shi'a claim that a certain verse (presumably 5:55) proves that Ali is the rightful imam and caliph after the Prophet. The author first presents the Shi'a argument: that the verse describes Ali as a 'wali', and if he is the one in charge of the imamate, it indicates he is the caliph. Nasafi responds with several points. First, if the verse meant what they claim, it would not have been hidden from all the Companions and especially from Ali himself, and they would not have unanimously agreed on someone else's caliphate, nor would Ali have given allegiance to another. Second, the verse itself is not explicit proof (it is ambiguous). Third, if the Shi'a can interpret the verse as referring to Ali, others can interpret it differently. Fourth, even if the verse refers to Ali, the word 'wali' does not necessarily mean 'imam'; it commonly means a friend or supporter, and that description is not impossible for him, but it does not establish his imamate during Abu Bakr's time.

Key Terms
الولي
الإمامة
الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That verse 5:55 explicitly appoints Ali as the caliph immediately after the Prophet.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the verse is ambiguous and the word 'wali' does not mean 'imam'; moreover, the consensus of the Companions and Ali's own actions contradict this claim.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the verse of wilayah not prove Ali's imamate?
A) Because the verse was abrogated.
B) Because the word 'wali' does not necessitate imamate.
C) Because Ali himself rejected the interpretation.
D) Because Abu Bakr was mentioned in the verse.

Key Takeaway
The verse of wilayah does not prove Ali's imamate because the word 'wali' can mean friend, not ruler, and the consensus of the Companions supports Abu Bakr's caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse from the Quran, are you willing to accept that your personal understanding might be incomplete, and that the understanding of the earliest community carries authority? Which belief of yours would be most challenged if you applied this principle consistently?
?
Imagine you were one of the Companions at the time of the Prophet’s death. Would you have been swayed by a single verse that seemed to favor one person, or would you have followed the consensus of the community? What does your answer reveal about your current approach to controversial religious issues?
?
This passage shows that even if a verse refers to Ali, it does not prove his imamate. Are there times in your own life when you have assumed a proof is sufficient, when in fact it leaves room for other interpretations? How can you train yourself to examine evidence more humbly and thoroughly?

beginner
5 min
Refuting the Hadith 'Allahumma Wali Man Walahu'
Imam Nasafi refutes the Shi'a argument from the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu' and defends Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Shi'a interpretation of the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu' and defends Abu Bakr's relationship with Ali.

Core Idea
The hadith is a prayer for virtue, not a designation of succession.

This passage presents a classical Sunni rebuttal to a key Shi'a proof text. Imam Nasafi argues that the famous hadith does not establish Ali's right to immediate succession, but merely expresses the Prophet's prayer for him. He then addresses the implication that Abu Bakr would be an enemy of God, citing evidence from Ali himself to show there was no enmity between them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shi'a claim
Read the first part of the passage to see what the Shi'a argue from the hadith. They claim it designates Ali as successor and implies Abu Bakr is an enemy.
2
Understand Nasafi's first refutation
Nasafi responds that the hadith is a prayer (du'a) and a demonstration of virtue, not a proof of succession (istikhlaaf).
Notice the distinction between prayer and designation.
3
Examine the counter-argument
The Shi'a then say: since Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, he is included in the prayer's curse. Nasafi denies this hostility.
This is the crux of the dispute.
4
See Nasafi's rebuttal and evidence
Nasafi asserts that Abu Bakr never wronged Ali, and if he had, the Companions would not have accepted him. He then cites Ali's own words that praise Abu Bakr and show harmony.
The evidence from Ali is key to the refutation.
5
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this argument defends the Sunni position on the caliphate and the integrity of the Companions.

مناقشة كلامهم في حديث اللهم" "وال ثم بيان معنى الولي] ولا حجة لهم فيما يحتجون به من قولهم عليه السلام: "اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه (۲) لأن الحديث ليس فيه دليل استخلافه بل دعاء له وإظهار لفضيلته. يحققه إن كان من يواليه الله تعالى لا يكون إمامًا، فإن قالوا: إنه عليه السلام قال: عاد" من عاداه وأبو بكر عادى عليا فيكون الله تعالى معاديًا له بقضية (۳) هذا الحديث. قلنا: إن أبا بكر لم يعاد عليا ولا غصب حقا له، ولو كان فعل ذلك لما اجتمعت معه الصحابة ولا بايعه علي له، فهذا من الروافض طعن على خيار الناس وصدور الأمة ونقله الدين باتباعهم من عاداه الله تعالى بدعاء رسوله، فطعن على علي وأتباعه من صار بمعاداته عدوا الله تعالى ثم قد ثبت عن علي أنه قال: حدثني أبو بكر، وصدق أبو بكر، وروي عن علي الله عنه أنه قال: قال النبي عليه السلام لأبي بكر: يا صديق الشرك أخفى (۱) في أمتى من دبيب النمل على الصفا في ليلة وغير ذلك من أحاديث (۳) تدل على أنه لم يكن بين أبي بكر وعلي معاداة.
Discussion of their statement regarding the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu...' and then clarification of the meaning of 'wali'. They have no proof in what they adduce from his saying (peace be upon him): 'O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him and be hostile to whoever is hostile to him,' because the hadith does not contain evidence of his succession, but rather a prayer for him and a demonstration of his virtue. That is affirmed: if the one whom God befriends does not become an imam, then they say: He (peace be upon him) said: 'Be hostile to whoever is hostile to him,' and Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, so God would be hostile to him according to this hadith. We said: Abu Bakr was not hostile to Ali, nor did he usurp any right of his. Had he done that, the Companions would not have gathered around him, nor would Ali have given him allegiance. This is from the Rafidah, slandering the best of people and the leaders of the community, and the transmission of the religion is through following those whom God has made hostile by the prayer of His Messenger. Thus they slander Ali and his followers, who by opposing him became enemies of God. Then it is established from Ali that he said: 'Abu Bakr narrated to me, and Abu Bakr spoke the truth.' And it is narrated from Ali (may God be pleased with him) that he said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Abu Bakr: 'O truthful one, polytheism is more hidden in my community than the crawling of ants on a rock in a dark night,' and other hadiths indicating that there was no hostility between Abu Bakr and Ali.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shi'a argument that the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu' proves Ali's right to imamate and implies that Abu Bakr, who supposedly opposed Ali, is an enemy of God. The author begins by citing the Shi'a claim that this hadith designates Ali as successor. Nasafi responds by pointing out that the hadith is merely a prayer for Ali and a demonstration of his virtue, not a designation of succession. He then addresses the Shi'a objection that since Abu Bakr was hostile to Ali, he would be included in the prayer's curse. Nasafi denies that Abu Bakr was ever hostile to Ali or usurped his rights; if he had, the Companions would not have accepted him as caliph, and Ali would not have given him allegiance. He accuses the Rafidah (Shi'a) of slandering the best of the Companions. He supports his argument by citing traditions from Ali himself, in which Ali praises Abu Bakr as truthful and narrates a hadith where the Prophet warned Abu Bakr about hidden polytheism, as well as other hadiths that show there was no enmity between them.

Key Terms
اللهم وال من والاه
المعاداة
الروافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu' proves that anyone who opposed Ali (like Abu Bakr) is an enemy of God.
Correction: Nasafi explains the hadith is a prayer, not a declaration of enmity, and cites evidence that Abu Bakr never opposed Ali.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the claim that Abu Bakr was an enemy of Ali based on the hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu'?
A) He says the hadith is weak.
B) He says Abu Bakr never opposed Ali and cites Ali's own statements.
C) He says the hadith applies only to the Prophet's lifetime.
D) He says the hadith was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Allahumma wali man walahu' does not prove Ali's immediate succession nor Abu Bakr's enmity; the Companions' consensus and Ali's own statements confirm their brotherhood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear arguments that pit Companions against one another, do you immediately side with one group based on prior loyalties, or do you seek out the actual evidence like Nasafi does here? What would it cost you to be honest even when it challenges your affiliations?
?
This passage shows Ali himself affirming Abu Bakr's truthfulness. Are you willing to let the words of the people involved, rather than later sectarian narratives, shape your view of early Islamic history? How would your heart change if you truly accepted that the Companions were united in brotherhood?
?
Nasafi calls slandering the Companions a form of 'slandering the best of people'. When you speak about the Companions or early Muslims, do you speak with the reverence due to those whom Allah chose to transmit the religion, or do you allow casual criticism? What is one step you will take today to guard your tongue regarding the Sahaba?

Refuting the Hadith 'Man Kuntu Mawlahu'
Nasafi refutes the Shi'a argument by clarifying the ambiguity of 'mawla'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Shi'a claim from the hadith by analyzing the multiple meanings of 'mawla'.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' has multiple meanings, so it cannot prove political succession.

The hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu' is often cited as proof of Ali's imamate. However, Imam Nasafi argues that the term 'mawla' is ambiguous, meaning 'helper' or 'cousin' in different contexts, and thus does not necessarily imply leadership. Let's examine his linguistic and contextual argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the Shi'a claim, Nasafi's response, and then the linguistic evidence.
The translation is literal; some terms may require explanation.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'mawla'
Find the two meanings Nasafi provides for 'mawla' – 'helper' and 'cousin'. Notice how he supports each with Quranic verses and poetry.
The first meaning is supported by Quran 66:4 and a line from al-Akhtal; the second by Quran 19:5 and a line from al-Fadl ibn Utbah.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the ambiguity of 'mawla' weakens the Shi'a argument. How does Nasafi's use of consensus (ijma') further support his position?
Consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate shows the Companions did not interpret the hadith as a designation of Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: روى عن النبي أنه قال: من كنت مولاه فعلی مولاه () فهذا يدل على إمامته. قلنا لو كان في الحديث دليل ذلك لما انعقد الإجماع على خلافة غيره، ثم المولى يذكر ويراد به الناصر على ما قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ هُوَ مَوْلَنَهُ وَجِبْرِيلُ وَصَلِحُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ (٦) وقال الأخطل :فأصبحت مولاها من الناس كلهم وأحرى قريش أن تهاب وتحمدا أي أصبحت ناصرها وحامي ذمارها (۱۷) و حافظ بيضتها، ويذكر ويراد به ابن العم قال الله تعالى خبرا عن زكريا عليه السلام: وَإِنِّي خِفْتُ الْمَوَالِيَ مِن وَرَاءِى ) (۸) وقال الفضل بن عتبة بن أبي لهب يخاطب بني أمية: مهلاً (1) بني عمنا مهلاً موالينا لا تنبشوا بيننا ما كان مدفونا
Then they say: It is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla' – this indicates his imamate. We said: If the hadith contained evidence of that, the consensus would not have been formed on the caliphate of someone else. Then, the word 'mawla' is used and intended to mean 'helper', as God Almighty said: 'Indeed, Allah is his Mawla, and Gabriel, and the righteous of the believers' (66:4). And al-Akhtal said: 'So I became her mawla from among all people, and the most deserving of Quraysh to be feared and praised.' That is, he became her helper and protector of her honor and guardian of her community. And it is used and intended to mean 'cousin', as God Almighty said, reporting from Zechariah (peace be upon him): 'And indeed I fear the mawali after me' (19:5). And al-Fadl ibn Utbah ibn Abi Lahab said, addressing the Banu Umayyah: 'Gently, O sons of our uncle, gently, O our mawali, do not dig up among us what was buried.'
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shi'a argument that the famous hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Ali mawlahu' proves Ali's right to imamate. The author first states the Shi'a claim that this hadith indicates Ali's imamate. Nasafi responds by pointing out that if the hadith truly designated Ali as imam, the Companions would not have unanimously agreed on the caliphate of someone else (i.e., Abu Bakr). Then he argues that the key term 'mawla' is ambiguous and has multiple meanings. He provides two specific meanings from linguistic and Qur'anic evidence: (1) 'helper' (nasir), citing the Qur'anic verse 66:4 where God is called the Mawla of the Prophet along with Gabriel, meaning He is his Helper. He also cites a line of poetry by al-Akhtal using 'mawla' in the sense of helper. (2) 'cousin' (ibn al-'amm), citing the Qur'anic verse 19:5 where Zechariah fears the 'mawali' (relatives) after him, and a poetic line where the poet addresses his cousins as 'mawali'. Thus, the hadith does not necessarily convey the meaning of succession or imamate; it could simply be a declaration of Ali's status as a helper or cousin of the Prophet. This refutes the Shi'a claim that the hadith explicitly appoints Ali as the imam.

Key Terms
المولى
الناصر
ابن العم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'mawla' in the hadith must mean 'master' or 'successor', ignoring its other valid meanings.
Correction: Nasafi shows 'mawla' means 'helper' (nasir) and 'cousin' (ibn al-'amm) using Qur'an and poetry.

Checkpoint
What two meanings of 'mawla' does Nasafi use to refute the Shi'a interpretation of the hadith?
A) Master and slave
B) Helper and cousin
C) Friend and enemy
D) Imam and deputy

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu' does not prove Ali's imamate because 'mawla' is ambiguous and can mean helper or cousin, not just master.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to an interpretation of a religious text because it supported your group or opinion, even when other meanings were possible? How does this lesson challenge you to be more objective in your study of scripture?
?
The Companions reached consensus on Abu Bakr despite the hadith about Ali. Do you trust the collective wisdom of the early Muslims more than your own understanding? What does that imply about how you approach disagreements in the Muslim community today?
?
Nasafi uses Quranic verses and poetry to show that 'mawla' has multiple meanings. When you encounter an ambiguous term in a hadith, do you actively seek its full semantic range, or do you settle for the most common meaning? How can you train yourself to examine linguistic evidence before forming a firm belief?

Refuting the Shia with Consensus and Linguistic Analysis of 'Mawla'
Nasafi refutes the Shia interpretation using consensus and linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' cannot indicate imamate based on the argument of consensus and the multiple meanings of the word 'mawla'.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' has many meanings; none support political succession.

The Shia rely on the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' to argue for Ali's immediate leadership. Nasafi counter-argues that if the hadith meant that, the early community would not have agreed on Abu Bakr's caliphate. He then examines the word 'mawla' in Arabic, showing it can mean helper, cousin, friend, neighbor, or son-in-law—none of which imply leadership. This lesson presents the consensus argument and the linguistic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the flow from objection to linguistic analysis.
2
Identify the consensus argument
Find where Nasafi says that if the hadith indicated imamate, consensus would not have formed on Abu Bakr's caliphate. Understand the logical force of ijma'.
This is a key methodological point in Sunni theology.
3
Examine the meanings of 'mawla'
The passage enumerates five meanings: helper, cousin, friend, neighbor, son-in-law. For each, note the Quranic or poetic evidence provided.
Notice the use of pre-Islamic poetry as linguistic evidence.
4
Understand why none support imamate
Nasafi concludes that none of these meanings can be the basis for caliphate or imamate. Reflect on how linguistic precision resolves the apparent conflict.
5
Reflect on the role of ijma'
Consider how consensus functions as a safeguard against misinterpretation of texts.
This is a distinctive feature of Sunni methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكر: يا صديق الشرك أخفى في أمتي من دبيب النمل على الصفا في ليلة وظلماء وغير ذلك من أحاديث تدل على أنه لم يكن بين أبي بكر وعلي معاداة. فإن قالوا روي عن النبي أنه قال: من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه فهذا يدل على إمامته. قلنا لو كان في الحديث دليل ذلك لما انعقد الإجماع على خلافة غيره، ثم المولى يذكر ويراد به الناصر على ما قال الله تعالى: {فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ هُوَ مَوْلَاهُ وَجِبْرِيلُ وَصَالِحُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ} وقال الأخطل : فأصبحت مولاها من الناس كلهم وأحرى قريش أن تهاب وتحمدا أي أصبحت ناصرها وحامي ذمارها وحافظ بيضتها، ويذكر ويراد به ابن العم قال الله تعالى خبرا عن زكريا عليه السلام: {وَإِنِّي خِفْتُ الْمَوَالِيَ مِن وَرَائِي} وقال الفضل بن عتبة بن أبي لهب يخاطب بني أمية: مهلاً بني عمنا مهلاً موالينا لا تنبشوا بيننا ما كان مدفونا ويذكر ويراد به الولي، والمحب الموالي، وقد روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: مزينة وجهينة وأسلم وعقار موالي الله ورسوله أي محبون موالون الله ورسوله، ويذكر ويراد به الجار. قال مربع بن دعدعة الكلابي - وكان جاور كليب بن يربوع فأحمد جوارهم جزى الله خيرا والجزاء يكفه كليب بن يربوع وزادهم حمدا هم حاطونا بالنفوس وألجموا إلى نصر مولاهم مسومة جردا ويذكر ويراد به الصهر قال المختار بن يزيد بن قيس الكلابي فلا تنسين النافعين كليهما وهذا الذي في السوق مولى بني بدر وكان الرجل صهرا لبني بدر وشيء من هذه المعاني لا تبنى على الخلافة والإمامة ثم ما يليق بالحديث من هذه المعاني: "الناصر"، أي من كنت ناصره على دينه وحاميا له بباطني وظاهري فعلي ناصره وحاميه بباطنه وظاهره؛ فيكون دليلاً على طهارة سيرة علي الله وعلو رتبته إذ ليس كل ناصر بظاهره هذا رتبته في باطنه إذ قد يفعل ذلك طلبًا للرياء والسمعة وابتغاء المال والفوز بالغنائم، ويحتمل أن يكون معناه من كنت محبوبًا عنده فعلي محبوب عنده، وفائدة تخصيصه بذلك أنه عليه السلام ربما علم أن قوما ممن أضلهم الله تعالى وأعمى أبصارهم سيطعنون عليه، ويزعمون أنه خرج من الدين وفارقه، وحكّم في أمر الله غير الله وسقطت بذلك ولايته وزال ولاؤه، فقال عليه السلام ذلك ليدل على بطلان قولهم
Abu Bakr: O truthful one, hidden polytheism in my nation is more hidden than the crawling of ants on a rock in a dark night, and other hadiths that indicate that there was no enmity between Abu Bakr and Ali. For if they say: It was narrated from the Prophet that he said: "For whomever I am his master, Ali is his master." This indicates his imamate. We say: If the hadith contained evidence for that, then consensus would not have been established on the caliphate of someone else. Then "mawla" is mentioned and intended to mean the helper, as God the Exalted said: {Indeed, God is his protector, and Gabriel and the righteous of the believers.} And al-Akhtal said: "So I became her mawla among all people, and the most deserving of Quraysh that they be feared and praised," meaning I became her helper, protector of her honor, and preserver of her integrity. And it is mentioned and intended to mean the cousin, as God the Exalted said, reporting from Zakariya, peace be upon him: {And indeed I fear the relatives after me.} And al-Fadl ibn Utba ibn Abi Lahab said, addressing the Banu Umayya: "Gently, O sons of our uncle, gently our patrons/mawali, do not dig up what was buried among us." And it is mentioned and intended to mean the friend, the loving ally. And it was narrated from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he said: "Muzayna, Juhayna, Aslam, and Ghifar are the allies/mawali of God and His Messenger," i.e., lovers and allies of God and His Messenger. And it is mentioned and intended to mean the neighbor. Marba' ibn Du'da'a al-Kilabi said—and he neighbored Kulayb ibn Yarbu' and praised their neighborliness: "May God reward with good, and the reward suffices, Kulayb ibn Yarbu', and increase them praise. They protected us with their lives and bridled for the victory of their master/mawla marked steeds." And it is mentioned and intended to mean the son-in-law. Al-Mukhtar ibn Yazid ibn Qays al-Kilabi said: "Do not forget the two benefactors, both of them, and this one in the market is the mawla of Banu Badr." And the man was a son-in-law of Banu Badr. And none of these meanings can be built upon the caliphate and imamate. Then what is suitable for the hadith from these meanings is "the helper": i.e., whoever I am his helper in his religion and his protector inwardly and outwardly, then Ali is his helper and protector inwardly and outwardly; so it is evidence for the purity of Ali's conduct, may God exalt him, and the loftiness of his rank, since not every outward helper has this rank inwardly, for one might do that seeking hypocrisy and reputation and seeking wealth and the booty. And it is possible that its meaning is: whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him. And the benefit of specifying him with that is that he, peace be upon him, perhaps knew that some people, among those whom God the Exalted led astray and blinded their eyesight, would revile him, claiming that he left the religion and forsook it, and judged by other than God in God's matter, and thus his love/authority fell and his allegiance ceased. So he, peace be upon him, said that to indicate the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Shia object by quoting the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu' (For whomever I am his master, Ali is his master) and claim it proves Ali's immediate imamate. Nasafi refutes this by first noting that if the hadith indicated that, the early community would never have achieved consensus (ijma') on the caliphate of Abu Bakr. Since ijma' occurred, the hadith cannot bear that meaning. He then provides a detailed linguistic analysis of the word 'mawla' (مولى), showing it has multiple meanings: helper (nasir), cousin (ibn al-amm), friend/loving ally (wali, muhibb), neighbor (jar), son-in-law (sihr). He supports each meaning with Quranic verses and pre-Islamic poetry. He concludes that none of these meanings can be the basis for caliphate or imamate. Finally, he offers two plausible interpretations consistent with Sunni theology: (1) 'helper'—the Prophet made Ali his helper in religion both inwardly and outwardly, indicating Ali's virtue but not succession; (2) 'beloved'—the Prophet declared that whoever loves him must love Ali, as a preemptive refutation against those who would later slander Ali and claim he left the faith. Thus, the hadith is about love and support, not political leadership.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla)
إمامة (imama)
خلافة (khilafa)
ناصر (nasir)
إجماع (ijma')
ولي (wali)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'mawla' in the hadith means political leader or successor.
Correction: The word 'mawla' has multiple meanings, and the consensus of the community shows it cannot mean political succession.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the Shia interpretation of the hadith based on consensus?
A) Because the hadith is weak in transmission.
B) Because if the hadith indicated imamate, the community would not have agreed on Abu Bakr's caliphate.
C) Because Ali himself rejected the caliphate.
D) Because the hadith was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The word 'mawla' is ambiguous; it cannot prove political leadership, especially when the community's consensus contradicts such an interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith like 'man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Ali mawlahu', do you automatically assume you know its meaning, or do you pause to consider the linguistic and historical context as Nasafi does? Will you commit to studying unclear texts with the same rigor before forming beliefs?
?
The early Muslim community achieved consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate despite this hadith. Does your own heart submit to the consensus of the sahaba, or do you secretly think they were mistaken? Ask yourself: would you have trusted their judgment better than your own?
?
The word 'mawla' can mean helper, cousin, friend, neighbor, or son-in-law — but none of those imply political rule. Are there any verses or hadith you currently interpret in a way that might be forced or biased? What would it cost you to re-examine them honestly?

The Sunni Interpretations of 'Man Kuntu Mawlahu'
Nasafi proposes two Sunni interpretations of the hadith related to support and love.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two plausible Sunni interpretations of the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' as indicating support and love, and the wisdom behind the Prophet's statement.

Core Idea
The hadith is about Ali's spiritual rank and the need to love him, not political succession.

After dismissing the political meaning, Nasafi provides two positive interpretations of the hadith. The first takes 'mawla' as helper (nasir), emphasizing that the Prophet supported Ali both inwardly and outwardly. The second takes it as beloved (muhibb), indicating that love for the Prophet entails love for Ali. He also explains the wisdom: the Prophet foresaw future slander against Ali and preemptively refuted it. This lesson explores these interpretations and their significance in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. This section is shorter and focuses on positive meanings.
Contrast this with the previous lesson's linguistic analysis.
2
Understand the first interpretation: helper
Nasafi interprets 'mawla' as helper. The Prophet said: Whoever I am his helper, then Ali is his helper. This highlights Ali's inner purity and high rank.
The inward-outward distinction is important.
3
Understand the second interpretation: beloved
Nasafi interprets 'mawla' as beloved. The Prophet said: Whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him. This establishes love for Ali as part of faith.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
The Prophet specified Ali because he knew some would later slander him. The hadith thus serves as a refutation of those claims.
This shows the prophetic concern for Ali's reputation.
5
Connect to Sunni theology
The interpretations protect the unity of the Companions and affirm Ali's virtue without claiming political succession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكر: يا صديق الشرك أخفى في أمتي من دبيب النمل على الصفا في ليلة وظلماء وغير ذلك من أحاديث تدل على أنه لم يكن بين أبي بكر وعلي معاداة. فإن قالوا روي عن النبي أنه قال: من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه فهذا يدل على إمامته. قلنا لو كان في الحديث دليل ذلك لما انعقد الإجماع على خلافة غيره، ثم المولى يذكر ويراد به الناصر على ما قال الله تعالى: {فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ هُوَ مَوْلَاهُ وَجِبْرِيلُ وَصَالِحُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ} وقال الأخطل : فأصبحت مولاها من الناس كلهم وأحرى قريش أن تهاب وتحمدا أي أصبحت ناصرها وحامي ذمارها وحافظ بيضتها، ويذكر ويراد به ابن العم قال الله تعالى خبرا عن زكريا عليه السلام: {وَإِنِّي خِفْتُ الْمَوَالِيَ مِن وَرَائِي} وقال الفضل بن عتبة بن أبي لهب يخاطب بني أمية: مهلاً بني عمنا مهلاً موالينا لا تنبشوا بيننا ما كان مدفونا ويذكر ويراد به الولي، والمحب الموالي، وقد روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: مزينة وجهينة وأسلم وعقار موالي الله ورسوله أي محبون موالون الله ورسوله، ويذكر ويراد به الجار. قال مربع بن دعدعة الكلابي - وكان جاور كليب بن يربوع فأحمد جوارهم جزى الله خيرا والجزاء يكفه كليب بن يربوع وزادهم حمدا هم حاطونا بالنفوس وألجموا إلى نصر مولاهم مسومة جردا ويذكر ويراد به الصهر قال المختار بن يزيد بن قيس الكلابي فلا تنسين النافعين كليهما وهذا الذي في السوق مولى بني بدر وكان الرجل صهرا لبني بدر وشيء من هذه المعاني لا تبنى على الخلافة والإمامة ثم ما يليق بالحديث من هذه المعاني: "الناصر"، أي من كنت ناصره على دينه وحاميا له بباطني وظاهري فعلي ناصره وحاميه بباطنه وظاهره؛ فيكون دليلاً على طهارة سيرة علي الله وعلو رتبته إذ ليس كل ناصر بظاهره هذا رتبته في باطنه إذ قد يفعل ذلك طلبًا للرياء والسمعة وابتغاء المال والفوز بالغنائم، ويحتمل أن يكون معناه من كنت محبوبًا عنده فعلي محبوب عنده، وفائدة تخصيصه بذلك أنه عليه السلام ربما علم أن قوما ممن أضلهم الله تعالى وأعمى أبصارهم سيطعنون عليه، ويزعمون أنه خرج من الدين وفارقه، وحكّم في أمر الله غير الله وسقطت بذلك ولايته وزال ولاؤه، فقال عليه السلام ذلك ليدل على بطلان قولهم
Abu Bakr: O truthful one, hidden polytheism in my nation is more hidden than the crawling of ants on a rock in a dark night, and other hadiths that indicate that there was no enmity between Abu Bakr and Ali. For if they say: It was narrated from the Prophet that he said: "For whomever I am his master, Ali is his master." This indicates his imamate. We say: If the hadith contained evidence for that, then consensus would not have been established on the caliphate of someone else. Then "mawla" is mentioned and intended to mean the helper, as God the Exalted said: {Indeed, God is his protector, and Gabriel and the righteous of the believers.} And al-Akhtal said: "So I became her mawla among all people, and the most deserving of Quraysh that they be feared and praised," meaning I became her helper, protector of her honor, and preserver of her integrity. And it is mentioned and intended to mean the cousin, as God the Exalted said, reporting from Zakariya, peace be upon him: {And indeed I fear the relatives after me.} And al-Fadl ibn Utba ibn Abi Lahab said, addressing the Banu Umayya: "Gently, O sons of our uncle, gently our patrons/mawali, do not dig up what was buried among us." And it is mentioned and intended to mean the friend, the loving ally. And it was narrated from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he said: "Muzayna, Juhayna, Aslam, and Ghifar are the allies/mawali of God and His Messenger," i.e., lovers and allies of God and His Messenger. And it is mentioned and intended to mean the neighbor. Marba' ibn Du'da'a al-Kilabi said—and he neighbored Kulayb ibn Yarbu' and praised their neighborliness: "May God reward with good, and the reward suffices, Kulayb ibn Yarbu', and increase them praise. They protected us with their lives and bridled for the victory of their master/mawla marked steeds." And it is mentioned and intended to mean the son-in-law. Al-Mukhtar ibn Yazid ibn Qays al-Kilabi said: "Do not forget the two benefactors, both of them, and this one in the market is the mawla of Banu Badr." And the man was a son-in-law of Banu Badr. And none of these meanings can be built upon the caliphate and imamate. Then what is suitable for the hadith from these meanings is "the helper": i.e., whoever I am his helper in his religion and his protector inwardly and outwardly, then Ali is his helper and protector inwardly and outwardly; so it is evidence for the purity of Ali's conduct, may God exalt him, and the loftiness of his rank, since not every outward helper has this rank inwardly, for one might do that seeking hypocrisy and reputation and seeking wealth and the booty. And it is possible that its meaning is: whoever I am beloved to him, then Ali is beloved to him. And the benefit of specifying him with that is that he, peace be upon him, perhaps knew that some people, among those whom God the Exalted led astray and blinded their eyesight, would revile him, claiming that he left the religion and forsook it, and judged by other than God in God's matter, and thus his love/authority fell and his allegiance ceased. So he, peace be upon him, said that to indicate the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Shia object by quoting the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu' (For whomever I am his master, Ali is his master) and claim it proves Ali's immediate imamate. Nasafi refutes this by first noting that if the hadith indicated that, the early community would never have achieved consensus (ijma') on the caliphate of Abu Bakr. Since ijma' occurred, the hadith cannot bear that meaning. He then provides a detailed linguistic analysis of the word 'mawla' (مولى), showing it has multiple meanings: helper (nasir), cousin (ibn al-amm), friend/loving ally (wali, muhibb), neighbor (jar), son-in-law (sihr). He supports each meaning with Quranic verses and pre-Islamic poetry. He concludes that none of these meanings can be the basis for caliphate or imamate. Finally, he offers two plausible interpretations consistent with Sunni theology: (1) 'helper'—the Prophet made Ali his helper in religion both inwardly and outwardly, indicating Ali's virtue but not succession; (2) 'beloved'—the Prophet declared that whoever loves him must love Ali, as a preemptive refutation against those who would later slander Ali and claim he left the faith. Thus, the hadith is about love and support, not political leadership.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla)
ناصر (nasir)
محبوب (mahbub)
ولاية (wilaya)
خلافة (khilafa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' cannot have any meaning other than political leadership.
Correction: Sunni interpretations show that the hadith can mean helper or beloved, which align with Ali's spiritual rank and refute slander.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the wisdom behind the Prophet specifically mentioning Ali in the hadith?
A) To appoint him as the next caliph.
B) To refute future slander against Ali.
C) To honor the Quraysh tribe.
D) To establish a new religious law.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' affirms Ali's spiritual rank and refutes slander, not his political succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet foresaw that some would slander Ali. When you hear negative claims about the Companions, do you immediately investigate their source and the authentic meaning of related texts? Will you commit to doing so before forming an opinion?
?
The passage distinguishes between outward support (seeking reputation) and inward support (sincere). In your own life, are there relationships where your help is more about appearances than genuine care? What would it take for you to purify your intentions?
?
If you knew someone you love would be wrongly accused after you're gone, would you proactively defend them as the Prophet did? Who in your community right now needs that kind of preemptive defense? What will you do about it starting today?

Refutation of the Objection to the Helper Interpretation
Nasafi responds to an objection with a parallel argument and evidence from consensus and Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the Shia objection to the interpretation of 'helper' in the hadith, and how he uses a parallel argument, then appeals to consensus and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Shia objections often prove too much: demanding explicit wording would also disprove their own interpretation.

In this passage, Nasafi responds to a Shia objection that the hadith should have said 'a believer in secret and open' rather than just 'helper'. He turns the objection back on them with a parallel argument: if the hadith meant 'imamate', it would have said 'your Imam after me'. He then asserts that the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus and the Quran, while the Shia interpretation is rejected by both.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part: 'If the meaning was what you mentioned, it would have been fitting for him to say a believer in secret and open.' Understand the Shia critique.
2
Read the answer
Read Nasafi's response: 'And if the meaning was the text for imamate, the Prophet would have said your Imam after me.' See the parallel argument.
Notice the tu quoque structure.
3
Analyze the argument
Nasafi argues that demanding such explicit wording restricts the Prophet's choice of words, which is invalid. Then he presents evidence: consensus and Quran support the Sunni interpretation, not the Shia one.
This step shows the two layers of refutation.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on how this argument defends the Sunni understanding of the hadith about 'helper' and refutes the Shia claim of explicit imamate designation.
This connects to broader debates about succession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لكان ينبغي أن يقول على مؤمن السر والعلانية نقي السريرة. قلنا ولو كان المراد منه النص على الإمامة لقال النبي عليه السلام على إمامكم بعدي ثم هذا من باب الحجر على الرسول عليه السلام في الألفاظ فاسد ، ثم ما حملنا الحديث عليه من المعنى لا يرده الإجماع ودلالة الكتاب، وما حملتم الحديث عليه مردود بهما، والله الموفق.
For they said: If the meaning was what you mentioned, it would have been fitting for him to say 'a believer in secret and open, pure in heart.' We said: And if the meaning was the text for imamate, the Prophet peace be upon him would have said 'your Imam after me.' Then this is of the type of restricting the Messenger peace be upon him in wording, which is invalid. Then what we have interpreted the hadith as in terms of meaning is not rejected by consensus and the evidence of the Book, and what you have interpreted the hadith as is rejected by both. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The objectors (Shia) claim that if the hadith meant 'helper', the Prophet should have been more explicit and said 'a believer in secret and open'. The author responds with a parallel argument: if the hadith meant 'imamate', the Prophet would have been even more explicit and said 'your Imam after me'. Furthermore, he argues that requiring such explicit wording would unjustly restrict the Prophet's choice of words. He then asserts that the Sunni interpretation (helper) is supported by both the consensus (ijma') of the community and the evidence of the Quran, while the Shia interpretation contradicts both. The argument is structured as a tu quoque: the Shia objection applies equally to their own position, and their position lacks external support.

Key Terms
إجماع
دلالة الكتاب
نص على الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia objection assumes that explicit wording is always stronger, so their interpretation (imamate) is more plausible because the hadith is explicit about 'mawla'.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the same logic would require even more explicit wording for imamate, and that the explicit wording they demand is not present, so their own interpretation fails by the same standard.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi respond to the objection that the hadith should have said 'a believer in secret and open'?
A) He agrees and changes the interpretation.
B) He says the Prophet could have said 'your Imam after me' if he meant imamate.
C) He denies the hadith is authentic.
D) He says the objectors misquoted the hadith.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Shia objection by turning its logic against itself and appealing to consensus and Quranic support for the Sunni view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong belief, do you ever demand that others prove their view with explicit statements, without realizing that your own view would crumble under the same demand? How can you break this double standard in your heart?
?
This passage shows Nasafi turning the argument against the objectors. When was the last time you honestly turned your own argument against yourself? What did you discover? What would it cost you to do that today?
?
Both consensus and Quran support one interpretation; the other is rejected. Which authorities do you trust when your own reasoning conflicts with the community of believers? Will you submit to the consensus even when your heart resists?

Refutation of the Harun Hadith as Evidence for Imamate
Nasafi refutes the Shia use of the Manzilat Harun hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the 'manzilat Harun' hadith does not prove Ali's immediate caliphate according to Nasafi's refutation.

Core Idea
The Prophet's comparison of Ali to Aaron does not establish a right to rule; the Companions' actions prove it.

Nasafi here addresses a key Shia proof for Ali's imamate: the hadith where the Prophet says Ali is to him as Aaron was to Moses. The author argues that neither the Companions nor Ali himself understood this hadith as a claim to leadership. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia claim and Nasafi's rebuttal
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the Shia present the hadith as evidence, and Nasafi immediately criticizes their understanding.
The rebuttal begins with a sharp critique of the Rafidah's lack of understanding.
2
Examine the consensus argument
Nasafi points to the consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate. Since they knew the hadith best, their action shows it does not imply Ali's succession.
This is a key point: the Companions' practice is a commentary on the hadith.
3
Consider Ali's own behavior
Nasafi argues that if the hadith were proof, Ali would have used it early on. He only mentioned it later in disputes, and even then did not insist on it as evidence.
This shows that Ali himself did not see the hadith as decisive.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage ends mid-sentence, stating the hadith is 'abandoned' (matruk). Consider how this would further undermine the Shia claim.
The text is incomplete, but the argument is clear so far.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا روى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال لعلي: "أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي" وهذا دليل خلافته. قلنا: إنكم معشر الرافضة لقلة أفهامكم وبلادة أوهامكم تتعلقون بما لا حجة لكم فيه ولا دلالة فيه على ما ترون إثباته، ثم دلالة صحة ما ادعينا من انعدام دليل الإمامة في هذا الحديث إجماع الصحابة مع أنهم العارفون بأحاديث النبي عليه السلام ومعاني كلامه على خلافة أبي بكر ومساعدتهم إياهم على ذلك، ولو كان فيما ذكرتم دلالة ما رمتم لما عدلوا عنه، ولا ترك علي له الاحتجاج به وطلب ما هو أحق به من غيره على ما قررنا، وكذا في زمانه حتى نازعه غيره في الأمر الذي هو أولى به فاحتج بهذا الحديث، ولا تعلق به علمًا منه أنه لا دلالة له فيه. ثم الكشف عما ادعينا من عدم دلالة الحديث على ذلك أن هذا الحديث متروك
For they said: It is narrated from the Prophet peace be upon him that he said to Ali: 'Are you not pleased that you are to me in the position of Aaron from Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' And this is evidence of his caliphate. We said: You, group of Rafidah, due to your lack of understanding and dullness of minds, cling to something in which there is no proof for you and no evidence for what you seek to prove. Then the evidence of the correctness of what we claim of the absence of evidence for imamate in this hadith is the consensus of the Companions – while they are the knowers of the Prophet's hadiths and the meanings of his speech – on the caliphate of Abu Bakr and their assistance to them in that. Had there been any evidence in what you mentioned, they would not have deviated from it, and Ali would not have left off using it as proof and seeking what he was more entitled to than others, as we have established. Similarly in his time, until others disputed with him over the matter which he was more entitled to, he used this hadith as proof, but he did not cling to it, knowing that it contains no evidence. Then the explanation of our claim of the lack of evidence of the hadith for that is that this hadith is abandoned.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shia argument from the hadith 'manzilat Harun' (the position of Aaron). The objectors claim that the Prophet's statement to Ali – that he is to him as Aaron was to Moses, except prophethood – proves Ali's right to immediate caliphate. The author responds by first criticizing the Shia understanding as lacking proof. He then provides a multi-layered refutation: (1) The consensus (ijma') of the Companions, who best understood the Prophet's words, to elect Abu Bakr as caliph shows they did not interpret this hadith as indicating Ali's imamate. (2) If it were such a clear proof, Ali himself would have used it to claim his right from the beginning, but he did not; he only mentioned it later when others disputed with him, and even then did not insist on it as decisive evidence. (3) The author then begins to argue that the hadith is actually 'abandoned' (matruk) – weakened – but the text cuts off here. The argument uses both historical evidence (consensus) and the behavior of Ali himself to undermine the Shia claim.

Key Terms
الرافضة
خلافته
إجماع الصحابة
منزلة هارون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'manzilat Harun' hadith explicitly proves Ali's right to be the first caliph.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the Companions, who understood the hadith best, did not see it that way, and Ali himself did not use it to claim leadership until later disputes.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Nasafi use to show the hadith does not prove imamate?
A) The hadith is weak in authenticity.
B) The consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate and Ali's behavior.
C) The hadith only refers to prophethood, not caliphate.
D) The Prophet explicitly denied it.

Key Takeaway
The Manzilat Harun hadith does not establish imamate because the Companions and Ali himself did not treat it as such.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that you deeply cherish, are you willing to examine the evidence against it honestly, even if it means admitting you were wrong? What would it take for you to abandon a position you've held for years if the proof is insufficient?
?
The Companions, who knew the Prophet best, understood his words differently than the Shia claim. Do you trust the understanding of those who were closest to the source, or do you prefer your own interpretation? How does this affect your approach to learning and following the truth?
?
Ali himself did not insist on this hadith as proof for his right to rule. Are there arguments or proofs you cling to that those more knowledgeable than you have not used? Are you relying on something that even the main figure involved did not rely on? How can you humble yourself to follow the example of those with deeper understanding?

Refutation of an Objection on Wording of Hadith
Objection and answer regarding the precise wording of a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars refute objections about hadith wording concerning the imamate through logical reversal and appeal to consensus and the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Restricting the Prophet's wording is invalid; opponents' literal reading is refuted by higher authorities.

This passage presents a classic objection from the Rafidah regarding the wording of a hadith about the caliphate. The Sunni scholar turns the objection around by showing that if the hadith were meant to appoint an imam, the Prophet would have spoken more explicitly. This lesson explores the logic of this refutation and the reliance on consensus and Qur'anic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The Rafidah argue that if the hadith meant what Sunnis say, the Prophet would have used a different phrase. Understand their reasoning: they demand a specific wording.
2
Study the Sunni response
The Sunni scholar turns the objection around: by the same logic, if the hadith were meant to appoint an imam, the Prophet would have said 'your imam after me.' This exposes the flaw in restricting the Prophet's wording.
This shows that the objection is self-defeating.
3
Understand the broader principle
The response argues that it is invalid to force a particular wording on the Prophet (الحجر على الرسول). Additionally, the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus (إجماع) and the Qur'an (دلالة الكتاب), while the Rafidah interpretation is rejected by both.
This emphasizes that theological claims must be consistent with primary sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم لكان ينبغي أن يقول على مؤمن السر والعلانية نقي السريرة. قلنا: ولو كان المراد منه النص على الإمامة لقال النبي عليه السلام على إمامكم بعدي ثم هذا من باب الحجر على الرسول عليه السلام في الألفاظ فاسد، ثم ما حملنا الحديث عليه من المعنى لا يرده الإجماع ودلالة الكتاب، وما حملتم الحديث عليه مردود بهما، والله الموفق.
If they say: If the intended meaning was what you mentioned, he should have said 'upon a believer of secret and public, pure of inner self.' We say: And if the intended meaning was the text on the imamate, the Prophet (peace be upon him) would have said 'upon your imam after me.' Then this is a matter of restricting the Messenger in wording, which is invalid. Then what we have carried the hadith to in meaning is not rejected by consensus and the evidence of the Book, and what you have carried the hadith to is rejected by both. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding the wording of a hadith. The objectors argue that if the intended meaning was what the Sunni scholars claimed, the hadith should have used a specific phrase. The response turns the objection back: if the hadith were meant to appoint an imam, the Prophet would have said 'your imam after me.' This shows that restricting the Prophet's actual wording is invalid. Additionally, the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus and the Qur'an, while the Rafidah interpretation is refuted by both.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الحجر على الرسول
الإجماع
دلالة الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Rafidah's literal reading of the hadith is decisive and cannot be answered.
Correction: The Rafidah's reading is refuted by turning the same literal demand against them: if the hadith were a clear text for imamate, the Prophet would have said something more explicit. Additionally, the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus and the Qur'an, which overrule mere word choice.

Checkpoint
How does the Sunni scholar refute the objection about the hadith's wording?
A) By accepting the objection
B) By arguing that the Prophet would have said something more explicit if the Rafidah interpretation were correct
C) By denying the hadith
D) By changing the wording

Key Takeaway
The Rafidah's literal interpretation of the hadith is invalid because the Prophet would have made explicit mention of imamate if intended, and the Sunni interpretation is supported by consensus and the Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith, do you sometimes demand that it must say exactly what you expect to accept its meaning? How might this restrict the Messenger's words in your heart, and what would it take for you to submit to the intended meaning even when the wording is not what you expected?
?
The Rafidah used a specific phrase to object; the Sunni scholar turned the same logic against them. Are you willing to have your own arguments turned against you when seeking the truth? What would you do if someone used your own reasoning to show you were wrong?
?
The scholar concluded with 'Allah is the grantor of success.' In your pursuit of understanding the sources, do you truly rely on Allah for guidance, or do you trust your own reasoning alone? How can you combine sincere study with humble dependence on Allah?

Refuting the Hadith: Consensus and Apparent Meaning
Refutation using consensus and the principle of abandoned apparent meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the consensus of the Companions and the abandonment of the hadith's apparent meaning invalidate its use as proof for Ali's immediate succession.

Core Idea
A hadith whose apparent meaning is abandoned cannot prove what it doesn't even cover.

The Rafidah argue that the Prophet's statement to Ali about being like Aaron proves his right to caliphate. In this lesson, we examine the first part of the refutation: the Companions' consensus on Abu Bakr and the fact that the hadith's literal meaning is rejected by all, so it cannot support their claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's direct address to the Rafidah.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage presents two arguments: first, the consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr and Ali's own abandonment of the hadith; second, the principle that the hadith's apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if the Companions and Ali himself did not use this hadith to claim leadership, why should anyone else? And how can a hadith whose literal meaning is rejected prove something that is not even mentioned in its wording?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا روى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال لعلي: 'أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي' وهذا دليل خلافته. قلنا: إنكم معشر الرافضة لقلة أفهامكم وبلادة أوهامكم تتعلقون بما لا حجة لكم فيه ولا دلالة فيه على ما تريدون إثباته، ثم دلالة صحة ما ادعينا من انعدام دليل الإمامة في هذا الحديث إجماع الصحابة مع أنهم العارفون بأحاديث النبي عليه السلام ومعاني كلامه على خلافة أبي بكر ومساعدتهم إياهم على ذلك، ولو كان فيما ذكرتم دلالة لما عدلوا عنه، ولا ترك علي له الاحتجاج به وطلب ما هو أحق به من غيره على ما قررنا، وكذا في زمانه حتى نازعه غيره في الأمر الذي هو أولى به فاحتج بهذا الحديث، ولا تعلق به علمًا منه أنه لا دلالة له فيه. ثم الكشف عما ادعينا من عدم دلالة الحديث على ذلك أن هذا الحديث متروك الظاهر بالإجماع فيما يصح تناوله إياه، وما هو المتنازع فيه لا دلالة له في الحديث عليه البتة، والتعلق بكلام لإثبات ما يتناوله ظاهره عند كونه متروك الظاهر ليس بصحيح فكيف التعلق به لإثبات ما لا يتناوله ظاهره ولا لفظه بوجه من الوجوه؟! وبيان ذلك أن هارون عليه السلام كان أخا لموسى من أبيه وأمه، وكان شريكا له في النبوة وتلقي الوحي من الله تعالى، ولم يكن هو خليفة لموسى عليه السلام بعد وفاته لأنه مات قبل موسى بسنتين، وأن عليا رضي الله عنه لم تثبت له أخوة النبي عليه السلام لأبيه وأمه ولا شركته في النبوة والرسالة بقضية هذا الكلام، وإن كان ذلك ثابتا لهارون من موسى فكيف تثبت له الخلافة بعده ولم يكن ذلك ثابتا لهارون من موسى عليهما السلام؟ فدل أن الاستدلال به في غاية البطلان. ثم نقول: لو عرفتم مخرج الحديث لما تعلقتم به وذلك لأن سبب الحديث أن النبي عليه السلام لما خرج إلى غزوة تبوك استخلف عليا على المدينة، فأكثر أهل النفاق في ذلك، وزعموا أن النبي عليه السلام أبغضه وقالاه واستثقل صحبته، فاتبع علي رسول الله عليه السلام ولحق به وقال: يا رسول الله أتتركني مع الأخلاف فقال: 'أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي' أي: إني أستخلفتك بعد غيبتي عن المدينة عليها كما استخلف هارون عليه السلام حين غاب عن قومه لمناجاة ربه، وهذا يدل على رضاه باستخلافه على المدينة مدة غيبته عنها لا على أنه خليفته بعده كما في حق هارون عليه السلام، فإذا ليس فيه إثبات خلافته نصا ولا دلالة أيضا فإنه عليه السلام استخلف
If they say: It is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said to Ali: 'Are you not pleased to be to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' And this is evidence for his caliphate. We say: O group of the Rafidah, due to your lack of understanding and dullness of your minds, you cling to that in which there is no proof for you and no indication for what you want to establish. Then the indication of the correctness of what we claim regarding the absence of any evidence for the imamate in this hadith is the consensus of the Companions, while they are the knowers of the hadiths of the Prophet and the meanings of his speech, upon the caliphate of Abu Bakr and their assistance to him in that. If there were any indication in what you mentioned, they would not have turned away from it, nor would Ali have abandoned arguing with it and seeking what he was more entitled to than others, as we have established. Likewise, during his time, until someone else disputed with him in the matter which he was more entitled to, he argued with this hadith, and he did not cling to it knowing that it had no indication for him. Then the explanation of what we claim of the lack of indication of the hadith for that is that this hadith is abandoned in its apparent meaning by consensus in what can validly apply to it. And what is disputed has no indication in the hadith at all. And clinging to speech to prove what its apparent meaning covers when the apparent meaning is abandoned is not correct. So how can one cling to it to prove what its apparent meaning does not cover nor its wording in any way?! The explanation of that is that Aaron (peace be upon him) was a brother of Moses from his father and mother, and he was a partner with him in prophethood and receiving revelation from Allah, and he was not a successor of Moses after his death because he died two years before Moses. And Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) has not been established as a brother of the Prophet from his father and mother, nor his partnership in prophethood and messengership by virtue of this speech. If that was established for Aaron from Moses, how can the caliphate after him be established for him when that was not established for Aaron from Moses? So it shows that the inference from it is extremely invalid. Then we say: If you knew the occasion of the hadith, you would not cling to it. That is because the reason for the hadith is that when the Prophet went out to the expedition of Tabuk, he appointed Ali as his deputy over Medina. The hypocrites spoke much about that and claimed that the Prophet hated him, was disgusted with him, and found his company burdensome. So Ali followed the Messenger of Allah and caught up with him and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, are you leaving me with the women and children?' He said: 'Are you not pleased to be to me as Aaron was to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' Meaning: I appoint you as deputy after my absence from Medina over it, just as Aaron was appointed deputy when he was absent from his people for the private conversation with his Lord. This indicates his approval of his deputyship over Medina during his absence from it, not that he is his successor after him as in the case of Aaron. So there is no proof in it for his caliphate, neither textually nor by indication. And also he (peace be upon him) appointed [as deputy]...
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Rafidah claim that the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' proves Ali's immediate succession after the Prophet. The response begins by attacking the Rafidah's understanding, then provides multiple arguments: (1) The consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate shows that they saw no evidence in this hadith, and Ali himself did not use it to claim leadership. (2) The hadith's apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus, so it cannot be used to prove what it doesn't cover. (3) A detailed comparison: Aaron was a brother and co-prophet, but even he did not succeed Moses because he died earlier. Ali lacks these qualities, so the analogy fails. (4) The occasion of the hadith was the expedition of Tabuk, where the Prophet appointed Ali as deputy over Medina in his absence, not as successor after death. Thus, the hadith only supports deputyship during absence, not universal succession.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة
الظاهر المتروك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' proves Ali's immediate succession.
Correction: The hadith's apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus, and the Companions did not see it as proof for succession.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what shows that the hadith is not evidence for Ali's caliphate?
A) The hadith is weak in chain.
B) The Companions agreed on Abu Bakr's caliphate and Ali did not use it.
C) The hadith was abrogated later.
D) The hadith is only about Moses.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' consensus and the rejection of the hadith's apparent meaning show it cannot support a claim for immediate succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that the Prophet's own companions, and even Ali himself, did not use this hadith to claim immediate succession. Are you willing to submit your interpretations to the understanding of those who were closest to the Prophet? What is stopping you from doing so?
?
The author says the Rafidah cling to a hadith whose apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus, trying to prove what it does not even cover. When have you done something similar—used a text to support a position beyond what it actually says? How can you repent from that misuse of divine speech?
?
If you were in Ali's position, would you have argued with this hadith to claim your right? He did not. What does that teach you about the character required to seek truth over personal advantage? How can you embody that character today?

The Analogy with Aaron and the True Context
Refutation by dismantling the analogy and revealing the actual occasion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the comparison between Ali and Aaron fails because Aaron was not a successor after death, and that the hadith's context was deputyship during absence.

Core Idea
Aaron was not Moses' successor; the hadith only refers to deputyship during a specific absence.

The Rafidah draw an analogy: if Ali is like Aaron, he must be the successor. But this lesson shows the flaws: Aaron was a brother and co-prophet, yet he did not succeed Moses. Moreover, the hadith's occasion—the expedition of Tabuk—reveals it was about temporary deputyship, not permanent succession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's dismantling of the analogy step by step.
2
Compare the relationships
The passage compares Moses-Aaron with Prophet-Ali. Identify the qualities of Aaron (brother, prophet, etc.) and note that Ali does not share them.
3
Understand the occasion
The passage then explains that the hadith was said during the Tabuk expedition, where the Prophet appointed Ali as deputy over Medina while he was away. This shows it was about temporary deputyship, not succession after death.
This connects to the learning objective.

The Logical Refutation of the Manzila Hadith Interpretation
Author states interpretive principle and logical refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the apparent meaning of the Manzila hadith cannot be used to prove Ali's caliphate, based on the principle of abandoned apparent meaning and the actual implications of the comparison with Harun.

Core Idea
A text whose apparent meaning is abandoned cannot prove a disputed claim.

The author begins by laying down a fundamental principle of interpretation: when a text's apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus, it cannot be used to prove its literal sense. He then applies this to the Shi'i claim, showing that even if the hadith were literal, the comparison with Harun does not support succession after death.

Guided Path
1
Understand the interpretive principle
Read the first sentence where the author states that the apparent meaning is abandoned by consensus regarding what may be taken literally. This is key to the argument.
The principle is that when scholars agree to abandon the literal meaning, it cannot be used as evidence.
2
Analyze the comparison with Harun
Notice how the author contrasts the actual relationship between Harun and Musa with the claimed relationship between Ali and the Prophet. Harun was a brother and partner in prophethood, yet he did not succeed Musa.
Ali lacks both brotherhood and partnership in prophethood.
3
Conclude the logical invalidity
The author concludes that the inference by the hadith is extremely invalid. Reflect on why the logical argument is compelling.
Even if the comparison were exact, it would not prove succession.
